"0||Mr Robot star Rami Malek to play Freddie Mercury in Queen biopicRami Malek, the star of Mr Robot, is the latest actor who has signed on to play Freddie Mercury in the troubled biopic Bohemian Rhapsody. The actor \u2013 who won an Emmy for his performance as neurotic hacker Elliot Alderson in Mr Robot \u2013 will play the Queen frontman, with X-Men: Apocalypse director Bryan Singer in talks to direct. Bohemian Rhapsody has been in production for eight years, with Queen members Brian May and Roger Taylor on board as music producers. Earlier this year disagreements about the direction the film should take caused Sacha Baron Cohen \u2013 who was originally cast as Mercury \u2013 to walk away from the project. Speaking to US radio host Howard Stern in March, Baron Cohen said he disagreed with May and Taylor over how much of the film should be dedicated to Mercury. The comedian and actor said: \u201cA member of the band \u2013 I won\u2019t say who \u2013 said: \u2018You know, this is such a great movie because it\u2019s got such an amazing thing that happens in the middle.\u2019\u201d \u201cAnd I go: \u2018What happens in the middle of the movie?\u2019 He goes: \u2018You know, Freddie dies.\u2019 \u2026 I go: \u2018What happens in the second half of the movie?\u2019 He goes: \u2018We see how the band carries on from strength to strength.\u2019 \u201cI said: \u2018Listen, not one person is going to see a movie where the lead character dies from Aids and then you see how the band carries on.\u2019\u201d May responded by saying that Baron Cohen had acted like an \u201carse\u201d. \u201cWe had some nice times with Sacha kicking around ideas,\u201d he told the Mail on Sunday\u2019s Event magazine. \u201cBut he went off and told untruths about what happened.\u201d May had hoped Ben Whishaw, who he called a \u201creal actor\u201d, would take over the lead role of the film, which has a script from the Theory Of Everything writer Anthony McCarten. Earlier this year Malek won best lead actor in drama at the Emmys, beating the likes of Kevin Spacey (House of Cards) and Matthew Rhys (The Americans) for a win that many saw as a surprise. The film is scheduled to begin shooting early in 2017."
"0||Government pulls its funding for London concert hallPlans to build a \u201cworld-class\u201d concert hall in London have been thrown into disarray after the government pulled its funding on Friday. The project, which was proposed by the conductor Simon Rattle, was not affordable, the government said. The former chancellor George Osborne put up \u00a35.5m to produce a detailed business case for the London Concert Hall to be developed. That followed a call from Rattle, who is due to take up the role of principal conductor at the London Symphony Orchestra next year, to build a better facility in London. But Osborne\u2019s successors in the cabinet have decided that the project, also known as the Centre for Music, does not offer value for money for the taxpayer and is not affordable. It is understood that around \u00a31.25m has been spent so far, with the rest now returning to the exchequer. \u201cLondon is already home to world class culture and music venues, from the iconic Royal Albert Hall to the Barbican Hall and the Royal Festival Hall at the Southbank Centre,\u201d a government spokesman said. \u201c[It] provided funding to consider the business case for a new London concert hall to add to this mix, but has concluded that it does not currently offer value for money for taxpayers and is not affordable. Therefore it will no longer fund contributions to this project. We know this will disappoint those who are working hard to deliver this project and we wish them well with developing alternative plans for financing.\u201d Mark Boleat, policy chairman at the City of London Corporation, said: \u201cThis is very disappointing news. We believe the government should have waited to see whether a business case could have been developed before making this announcement.\u201d A spokesperson for the mayor of London said: \u201cThis is bad news for London. World-class cities need world-class music venues like the Centre for Music. This looks like a vote of no confidence in London from the government.\u201d"
"1||The Spectator hands out more of its sad awards \u2013 where was mine?Monday Spare a thought for Michael Shanly, a property developer who is either Britain\u2019s most incompetent tax evader or the most unlucky. If ever a man is entitled to feel he has been picked on it is Shanly, as a report from the National Audit Office has revealed he is the only person to be prosecuted by an HMRC crack unit of accountants set up in 2009 to pursue high-net-worth individuals thought to be paying less than their fair share of tax. To be fair to HMRC, the unit has only passed two cases on to the Crown Prosecution Service so it can claim a 50 % hit rate \u2013 though some credit for Shanly\u2019s conviction must go to the French authorities who passed on a list of about 6,000 British citizens who were squirrelling money away in HSBC bank accounts in Switzerland. Tuesday The rewards for failure can some times be quite high. Most people might have assumed Will Straw would have wanted to keep a low profile after the way he snatched defeat from the jaws of victory as the mastermind of the Stronger In campaign in June\u2019s EU referendum, but instead he was rewarded with a CBE in David Cameron\u2019s resignation honours list. Straw had this to say when asked what he had done to deserve the gong by Labour\u2019s Paul Flynn: \u201cI don\u2019t know why I was given the reward. I didn\u2019t ask for the award, but when I was offered it I wasn\u2019t going to turn it down, not least because I had been \u2013 as campaigners on both sides of this campaign had been \u2013 away from my family for a lot of the campaign, and I wanted to have an occasion to take my wife to the palace.\u201d As someone who was also away from home during the referendum , I\u2019m sure my gong will soon be in the post. Wednesday MPs may not be very forgiving of other people\u2019s weaknesses \u2013 scarcely a day goes by in the Commons without someone being outraged by something \u2013 but they are very indulgent towards each other. You might have thought that Michael Gove would have been sent to the backbenches indefinitely for the levels of treachery he displayed towards his friends during the EU referendum campaign, but the former justice secretary believes two months is quite enough for rehabilitation and has successfully got himself elected to the new Brexit select committee. Forgiveness can also be a two-way process: just months after Labour-MP-turned-washing-machine-salesman Keith Vaz stepped down from his position as chair of the home affairs select committee after he was alleged to have offered to stump up the cash to buy cocaine for a couple of sex workers, his comeback has begun. With the support of more than 150 Tory MPs who are keen that any future misdemeanours of theirs be treated with such quality of mercy, Vaz has been elected to the justice select committee. Pour encourager les autres. Or something. Thursday Last year the Spectator parliamentarian of the year awards were a \u2013 relatively \u2013 sober lunchtime affair at the Savoy hotel in London. This year the awards were moved to a gala evening dinner and my invitation sadly went awol. Must have been something I said last year. As you might expect, it is largely a bunfight for top Tories, though there are the few exceptions. Last year Diane Abbott collected Jeremy Corbyn\u2019s award for \u2018campaigner of the year\u2019; this year she did the same for John McDonnell. Next year she might even win the category herself for her willingness to appear any time, any place, anywhere. Boris Johnson won \u2018comeback of the year\u2019, though he rather spoiled it by declaring Brexit would be as big a success as the Titanic. I guess it all depends on whether you\u2019re one of the lucky ones in the lifeboat. The winner of the top accolade went to Theresa May, though the Speccy had been careful to relabel her category as politician of the year. Last year\u2019s winner was David Cameron. Remember him? Friday My local hospital, St George\u2019s in south-west London, is to be put in special measures after inspectors from the Care Quality Commission declared it to be unsafe and inadequately led. In its defence, I would like to claim what could be a world record for treatment at its A&amp;E department today. Having woken up at 3.30 in the morning with crippling pains in my back which felt awfully familiar to the kidney stones I had 12 years ago, I woke up my wife and got her to drive me to St George\u2019s. After no wait whatsoever, I was seen by a nurse, given pain relief and packed off to a cubicle. Within the next 90 minutes, I had had my blood taken, an ultrasound and a CAT scan performed, and by 9am I had been diagnosed with two kidney stones and sent back home with a packet of painkillers and the advice that they would either dissolve or pass within the next few days. Fingers crossed for the former. The moral of this \u2013 apart from a hospital in special measures also being a centre of excellence \u2013 is to get ill in the middle of the night. Digested week: Judges 1 \u2013 0 Brexit"
"2||With opponents inside and out, there's no plain sailing for Malcolm TurnbullI want to do something very simple this weekend to cut through the staging and the static in Canberra right now. I want to talk about what\u2019s happening rather than what people are saying. Over the next few weeks two things are likely to happen. The Turnbull government appears likely to unveil a deal that will allow some of the wretched souls detained in Nauru and Manus to finally be settled in other countries. Assuming no outbreak of extreme crazy (which is a big thing to assume in the current climate, I\u2019ll admit), it is also likely to ratify the agreement it signed up to in Paris last December, the one that produced commitments for all countries to achieve net zero greenhouse gas emissions and limit global warming to 1.5C to 2C. In normal political times, in that fantasy scenario where Malcolm Turnbull won the July election resoundingly, banked his personal mandate and was permitted to act more or less consistently as the politician voters have seen in the past, these things could be just transacted and presented to the public on their merits. But Turnbull has two problems. The first is that Labor is within striking distance of government. When you can feel the hot breath of your opponent on your neck, the backroom wisdom in such circumstances tends to dictate that daily politics becomes about maximising the points of difference with your opponent. We can call this survivalist mantra \u2013 pick a fight, any fight. The second problem has been on full display since the prime minister failed to win the election in the convincing way he wanted to win. Tony Abbott will play every point he thinks he can get away with, and even some he can\u2019t get away with. Even if he\u2019s a lone assassin, and right now he is, disowned by mostly everyone in the Coalition for different reasons, the voters see division and the net effect on a government is corrosive. Quite apart from the bruised feelings and antics of the prime minister in exile, the government remains divided philosophically and factionally, and the Coalition lacks Labor\u2019s institutional mechanisms to enforce internal discipline. Everything is contested. Nothing is straightforward. So instead of just producing these two developments, the third-country resettlement deal and the Paris agreement ratification, as straightforward achievements, as statements of what kind of government the Turnbull government aspires to be \u2013 a government that won\u2019t tolerate offending a basic principle of liberalism by keeping people arbitrarily detained offshore when they\u2019ve committed no crime, and a government that knows we have to deal with the existential threat of climate change \u2013 they have to be presaged by bouts of extremism. Hence the proposed bill banning asylum seekers from ever setting foot in Australia for the remainder of their days, because that will fire up the base, wrong foot your opponent and lull the Fox News Lite night shift on Sky News \u2013 just in case anyone had a transitory impulse to wonder if Peter Dutton had suddenly gone soft \u2013 which could easily trigger an outbreak of cognitive dissonance from Ray Hadley and then Dutton might have to find a Bible to swear on. Then there\u2019s all the shouting about windfarms, which makes absolutely no sense, given that Turnbull knows precisely what has to happen by way of transition to low-emission technologies and Josh Frydenberg is one of the brightest people in the parliament. Both of these characters know full well coal is on the way out, and Australia will need the much-maligned windfarms and the solar farms to decarbonise the grid in sufficient time to meet our international commitments, and to try to mitigate against the dangerous effects of climate change. Both were called out diplomatically, but comprehensively, on the political bollocks, in a speech the chief scientist made on Thursday night. Turnbull and Frydenberg, and the renewables bull horn do make a bit more sense if we tumble to the fact the government has not yet ratified the Paris deal, and doubtless wants to be able to ratify the Paris deal with only the mildest tut-tut from the Quadrant corner, rather than the whole process triggering yet another bout of internal cage fighting within the Coalition about climate change and whether it\u2019s happening or not. Frydenberg and the foreign minister, Julie Bishop, are going to the planned meeting of leaders in Marrakech, Morocco, which starts this week. This pow-wow has been convened to decide the global rules towards achieving net zero emissions, which was the core of the Paris agreement. In no rational universe does the Australian government want to be in a position of going to that meeting and telling other countries we don\u2019t intend to ratify the Paris agreement, but nothing is done until it\u2019s done and the process isn\u2019t yet locked down with a cabinet ruling. Obviously, this whole play \u2013 \u201clook at those dreadful renewables and, oh look, here\u2019s the Paris agreement\u201d \u2013 is a lot more complicated than a simple game of bait and switch. But for a bit of light relief at the weekend, you could think of this as a drawing room comedy in which the lead character says, \u201cMy, aren\u2019t windfarms terrible, Rupert,\u201d while Rupert placidly signs another piece of paper guaranteeing a proliferation of windfarms into the future. Thanks, Rupert. Rupert always was a good sort. Provided Rupert is a good sort, of course. On that score, we are going to have to watch and wait."
"3||Anthony Joshua vows to humble Eric Molina in IBF title fightA knockout machine in the ring, Anthony Joshua has developed a reputation as one of boxing\u2019s more genial figures out of it. So it came as a surprise to hear the heavyweight express his desire to make the Mexican American Eric Molina \u201clook like shit\u201d when the pair square up next month. Joshua faces the 34-year-old at the Manchester Arena on 10 December in what will be the second defence of his IBF title and Friday saw the beginning of promotional duties for both fighters, which to a large extent explains Joshua\u2019s attention-grabbing remark, because this is a fight that needs a fair amount of promoting. Molina, after all, is the replacement for Wladimir Klitschko, whom Joshua was set to meet in a little over a month\u2019s time before the Ukrainian picked up a \u201cminor injury\u201d in training. Molina is a game opponent and has an interesting story but he is no Klitschko. The sense of anticlimax is tangible. Joshua admitted as much when he described himself as a \u201clittle bit\u201d disappointed not to be taking on the 40-year-old former three-belt holder next. But with the pair expected to fight next spring after the WBA finally agreed to the \u201csuper\u201d version of their world heavyweight title also being up for grabs \u2013 a major stipulation for Klitschko \u2013 the 27-year-old insists he is fully focused on Molina and putting on a show against a man who has vowed to provide the 2012 Olympic champion with the toughest fight he has been in since turning professional. \u201cThey [opposition fighters] say they\u2019re going to give me the toughest fight of my life and if that\u2019s their mentality, I\u2019ll reverse it and make them look like shit,\u201d said Joshua, who has stopped each of the 17 men he has faced since winning gold in London, including Charles Martin, who he knocked out inside two rounds to become world champion seven months ago. \u201cIf I took him [Molina] 12 rounds but played with him, that would say I have a better skill set. If I knock him out early, that says I have better punching power. As long as I win in a good fashion I can tick a good box.\u201d Joshua was in noticeably relaxed mood as he spoke and put that down to having taken the longest break of his professional career following his seventh-round stoppage of Dominic Breazeale in June. That was the joint furthest anyone had taken Joshua and by the Briton\u2019s own admission he was not at his best going into what was his first defence of the IBF title, after a disrupted training camp and glandular fever. \u201cThe break after was a godsend,\u201d he said. \u201cMe and the lads had an unbelievable holiday \u2013 to Koh Samui in Thailand \u2013 and I only started getting back into boxing when I went to Rio [Olympics] to watch the fights out there. I came back a week or so later and have been back in the gym since.\u201d Joshua admitted he was preparing for Klitschko until two weeks ago but insists switching focus to Molina has not been problematic. \u201cThese things happen,\u201d he said. \u201cI just crack on and keep the ball rolling.\u201d That is also the case for Joshua\u2019s promoter, Eddie Hearn, who was at his hard-selling best at the press conference, hailing the quick sale of tickets for this fight (15,000 in five minutes, apparently). But there is no doubt he faces a challenge persuading the usual number of people to take up pay-per-view subscriptions now it is Molina and not Klitschko who is on the bill. The Texas-born fighter arrives in England having won 25 of his 28 professional bouts, 19 by knockout. But it says much about Molina\u2019s record that the most notable fight was a defeat to WBC champion Deontay Wilder in June last year. Molina rocked the undefeated 31-year-old with a left hook in the third round before Wilder recovered and finished off the job in trademark powerful fashion in the ninth. Molina believes that display, along with his most recent \u2013 the 10-round stoppage of two division world champion Tomasz Adamek in Poland on 2 April \u2013 shows he has the physical capabilities and mental strength to overcome Joshua, with the latter attribute backed up by the fact he has up until now combined boxing with being a full-time special-needs teacher in the Texan city of Edinburg. \u201cI\u2019ve no amateur experience but I\u2019m getting better in every fight,\u201d Molina said. \u201cI\u2019m here to make history.\u201d"
"4||Antonio Conte work ethic philosophy pays off with big productivity gainsThose Chelsea players still on extended leave in mid-July after the European Championship need only have spied snippets of Antonio Conte\u2019s inaugural press conference to discern the new head coach\u2019s mantra. He used the word \u201cwork\u201d, or one of its derivatives, 32 times in a little under an hour that day, including one response where he insisted he was \u201ca worker who likes to work\u201d. That was followed up with a rat-a-tat \u201cI only know this verb: to work, work, work\u201d, which was less a reflection on his English and more a reminder of the philosophy which would restore this club\u2019s reputation. At the time it felt like a threat to his adopted playing staff. It may actually have been a warning for the rest of the Premier League. Fast-forward through a draining pre-season littered with those dreaded triple sessions, undertaken from Cobham to the west coast of the United States, and intensive endurance work to improve fitness, or the stop-start and cluttered match scenarios throughout which Conte barks tactical instructions relentlessly from the sidelines, and Chelsea are reaping the benefits. Plenty has been made of the switch to a three-man defence at Arsenal six weeks ago, when his side trailed 3-0 and the Italian\u2019s brittle patience snapped. But, while that was a key tweak born of desperation, this team\u2019s recent progress reflects as much on the acceptance of a mindset. A playing staff that was not treated to a particularly radical summer overhaul has bought into the new man\u2019s demands. Sometimes acceptance has come grudgingly, with Diego Costa far from alone in finding the constant reminders of positional responsibilities or defensive workload rather grating. The Brazil-born striker has made his feelings rather more obvious than most with the occasional fit of pique on the turf, as demonstrated against Leicester last month. Many of these same players had grown to dread life under the intense Jos\u00e9 Mourinho regime, after all, and have not always taken easily to disciplinarian management. But nothing fuels conviction better than a run of victories. Chelsea welcome Everton on Saturday having won all four league games since that ignominy at the Emirates Stadium, and are approaching seven hours without conceding a goal in the top flight. This group of players has grown convinced that all the preparatory work that Fabio Quagliarella, a striker who played under Conte at Juventus, has described as \u201cback-breaking\u201d will get the best from them. \u201cWe thought things would be different but it was important we bought into what was happening\u201d said Gary Cahill. \u201cChange can be good. He\u2019d had success in the past and brought the ideas that he strongly believes in to our club. Early on it was difficult in terms of the fitness work. Now it\u2019s intense in the way we work in terms of tactics or analysis of games alongside that fitness work.\u201d The meticulous levels of detail to which Conte subjects his side, constantly stopping training to remind players if they have wandered even marginally out of position, might explain how this group have adopted a major change in formation apparently so seamlessly. There are no shortcuts written into his approach. Those who have been at Chelsea a while have suggested the Italian is the most demanding of all the club\u2019s recent managers, and the players must retire from Cobham with his bellowed instructions seared into their psyche. Conte chuckled when asked about the Ambrosoli al Miele throat lozenges he has been sucking through training and on the touchline to soothe a voice strained by constant shouting, before adding the key is a spoonful of honey taken just before kick-off and at half-time. \u201cMy wife tells me my voice is more attractive like this, more sensual,\u201d he said through a mock croak. \u201cBut I prefer to have my normal voice.\u201d The work ethic was always likely to be personified by N\u2019Golo Kant\u00e9\u2019s tireless running, but it is as much in evidence in the upturns being enjoyed by Cahill and David Luiz, or the leggy Nemanja Matic. The Serb is restored as the midfielder who rampaged over all-comers in the first half of the title-winning season. He acknowledged that \u201ceveryone feels one step ahead\u201d of where they were last year. That would apply to Costa, who has drawn the focus with his eight league goals but is deserving of more plaudits for the selfless way in which he has unsettled opposing defences. He remains one booking from a suspension, but he has been since the Arsenal game. There is even new-found self-restraint. Then there is Eden Hazard, a player who became consumed by his own inability to influence games positively last term but who has now scored in three successive matches for the first time in his career. \u201cWith only talent, it\u2019s difficult to be the best,\u201d said Conte. \u201cFootball has changed, above all in England where you play with an intensity that is supersonic, so you have to support talent with strength to cope with contact, speed and technique.\u201d The Belgian, increasingly won over to the Italian\u2019s methods as he witnesses the rewards first-hand, is finding that blend, though this is only the start. For the record, there were 26 utterances of \u201cwork\u201d or the like in a half-hour media briefing on Friday. Chelsea may have found their form but the message remains very much the same."
"4||Mauricio Pochettino haunted by Tottenham\u2019s poor end to last seasonMauricio Pochettino has said he continues to be bothered by how Tottenham Hotspur contrived to finish below Arsenal last season, as he weighed up a couple of key selection decisions for Sunday\u2019s derby at the Emirates Stadium. The Tottenham manager said Harry Kane, who has not played since the middle of September because of ankle ligament damage, will return to the squad and he gave the striker a 50-50 chance of making the starting lineup. In midfield, Mousa Demb\u00e9l\u00e9 is a doubt after twisting an ankle in the 1-0 Champions League home defeat by Bayer Leverkusen at Wembley on Wednesday and if he fails to prove his fitness Pochettino will consider handing the 20-year-old Harry Winks a first start in the Premier League. Pochettino said at the beginning of the season he had been angry all summer about the way Spurs had finished the previous campaign. Their title hopes ended with the 2-2 draw at Chelsea in the third-last game but they were still three points clear of Arsenal in second place. Tottenham have not finished above Arsenal since 1994-95 and it would have meant something to break the sequence, but they lost 2-1 at home to Southampton and then, needing a point on the final day to secure a runners-up finish behind Leicester City, they were thrashed 5-1 at relegated Newcastle United. Arsenal beat Aston Villa 4-0 to leapfrog them and maintain Ars\u00e8ne Wenger\u2019s record of never finishing below Spurs. \u201cI still feel bad about last season,\u201d Pochettino said. \u201cIt was so easy to be above Arsenal in that moment. It\u2019s true we felt very disappointed after the Chelsea game, but two games ahead and only one point to be above them [on the final day] \u2013 that was very frustrating and disappointing. It was difficult not to think about that situation. But we have another opportunity now and we will see what happens.\u201d Pochettino has won one and drawn three of his four league derbies against Arsenal \u2013 he lost to them in last season\u2019s Capital One Cup \u2013 and his mind went back to the first of them when Winks was discussed. In September 2014, after Demb\u00e9l\u00e9 had been ruled out of the visit to the Emirates, Pochettino decided to start with Ryan Mason, then 23 years old. It was his debut in the Premier League and he played well in the 1-1 draw. Pochettino has never been afraid to put his trust in young players \u2013 even in high\u2011pressure games \u2013 and Winks is the one who has impressed him the most this season; more than Cameron Carter-Vickers and Josh Onomah. Pochettino was delighted with some of Winks\u2019s performances in pre-season and he has since introduced him as a late substitute in three league matches. Winks started both of the club\u2019s EFL Cup ties \u2013 the first the 5-0 home win against Gillingham and the second the 2-1 loss at Liverpool \u2013 and there was a further show of faith in him during the Leverkusen defeat. After Tottenham had conceded after 65 minutes at Wembley, Pochettino brought on Winks for Christian Eriksen as he chased the game. There is the feeling that with Tottenham at a low ebb after Leverkusen \u2013 they have not won in six games \u2013 the manager may benefit from shaking up the team. Could Winks, whom Pochettino prefers in a deeper-sitting role, be a solution in the starting XI? \u201cMaybe,\u201d Pochettino said. \u201cWe need to assess Demb\u00e9l\u00e9, first of all, to decide but Winks is doing very well \u2013 not only against Leverkusen but Liverpool in the cup, before. \u201cFrom pre-season, you remember in Australia, he played very well against Atl\u00e9tico Madrid and Juventus. In that moment he started to build his future and I started to see we can trust in him. This is very good news for us.\u201d Pochettino was reminded of the parallel with Mason in the derby at the Emirates. \u201cMaybe we will repeat that,\u201d he said. \u201cIt\u2019s a coincidence, because there were different reasons [with Mason] but, maybe, Winks has the opportunity to play. It\u2019s one of the possibilities.\u201d Kane has long been pencilled in to make his comeback in the derby and earlier this week Pochettino suggested it was more than likely the striker would be a substitute. He has now raised the prospect of him starting \u2013 after Kane made a positive return to full training on Thursday. \u201cIt is true that on Wednesday I was positive but not very, but after Thursday I am more positive,\u201d Pochettino said. \u201cWill he start? It\u2019s 50-50.\u201d"
"4||The evolution of Arsenal\u2019s Mesut \u00d6zil earns comparison with BergkampBasking in the glow of the masterpiece match-winner that was the talk of Europe this week, Mesut \u00d6zil made his way serenely, contentedly, back towards London. It seemed as if half of Bulgaria wanted a bit of him before he left. He gladly had time for everyone \u2013 finessing every photo, every signature, every word of appreciation with a special touch. It was not so long ago this particular player\u2019s body language was routinely picked apart. He was too languid. Too casual. Did not want to know. Wilted under pressure. All softness without the required substance. Too many big games passed him by ... but those criticisms have faded into the shadow of an emboldened talent who is relishing the spotlight. The \u00d6zil of today carries himself differently. The composure and confidence represents how he is enjoying the feeling of being centre stage. That is what is expected of him on Sunday, in the heat of the north London derby, where the early title credentials of both teams will undergo a particularly spicy examination. Tottenham have ruffled Arsenal at the Emirates in the last couple of seasons, requiring late equalisers from the home team to save face. It promises to be as frenetic and fierce as usual, which is not the stereotypical idea of \u00d6zil\u2019s favourite environment. Yet there is more obvious determination in him to want to influence games these days. \u201cHe pushes himself much more,\u201d Ars\u00e8ne Wenger says. For a start he is much more muscular than the slender figure who arrived at Arsenal, discarded by Real Madrid, three years ago. It has been known for defenders to bounce off him this season, a far cry from the elegiac shrug he used to give when unsettled by some opposing hulk. His reinvention as a player who has added a striker\u2019s runs to his playmaking passes looks intriguingly transformative. \u00d6zil the goalscorer? Who would have thought it? Well, Wenger did. It took some time, and some encouragement but \u00d6zil\u2019s adjusted position, as a high 10 rather than a deep-lying creator, makes him an even more effective performer. \u201cI convinced him that he has it in his locker to score goals,\u201d Wenger says. \u201cHe was more focused on giving and looking. Today he understands he can have those qualities but as well be on the end of things. The timing of his runs is good. In training I noticed he is a very good finisher. When you have that in your locker you have to try to get it out. Slowly, he now realises that. Now he makes more runs to score goals. On top of that he has a fantastic attribute of a guy who is a good finisher \u2013 he is calm. You notice all the finishers are killers. That means they keep their nerve in front of goal and he has that.\u201d The old Dennis Bergkamp comparison has been aired recently. Wenger sees the similarities and is urging his current master of technique to do what it takes to earn the kind of status Bergkamp enjoyed at Arsenal. \u201cHe can be a legend if he commits,\u201d Wenger says. \u201cDennis Bergkamp committed for a long period, he played until he was 38. I wish we have 10 more years of \u00d6zil. To become a legend at the club you need to stay for a long time. If you stay two years you would not be a legend.\u201d Arsenal are doing their best on the contract front to secure an extension. \u201cWe work on it,\u201d Wenger said, accentuating the positive sign of how content \u00d6zil seems on and off the pitch. \u201cA player needs to meet his needs inside a club. It is difficult not to be happy in London and happiness that a player has when he plays comes out by his performance.\u201d It is typical of how \u00d6zil has come out of his shell that he is keen to be a face of the club more readily. In this weekend\u2019s buildup, he took part in a social media Q&amp;A session. The hashtag #AssistMeMesut invited fans who needed some help with anything to ask Arsenal\u2019s No11. Some of the requests were unsurprisingly left-field. \u00d6zil filmed answers to a handful of questions, ranging from a Turkish restaurant recommendation to relationship advice. \u201cSo my friend,\u201d \u00d6zil told @theDjole, \u201cJust be nice to her, show her your qualities and I wish you all the best.\u201d Wenger remembers the day well when \u00d6zil arrived in a flurry of deadline day activity in September 2013. \u201cIn fact on the day we signed him we played against Spurs,\u201d he says, breaking into a grin. \u201cWe won without him.\u201d It was a landmark signing for the club, at \u00a342m demolishing its transfer record. \u00d6zil has grown into life at Arsenal. Even for a player with his experience and the expectation that comes with such a high-impact signing, it was a slow burn. The efforts that have been a hallmark this season took some time to develop. Wenger acknowledges the Premier League was a bit of a culture shock for \u00d6zil. \u201cHe left some people sceptical \u2013 saying he didn\u2019t go too much into challenges or tough contact. [He punches his hand for emphasis.] He\u2019s a player who is more a guy whose strength is to get out of things but on the physical front I believe he\u2019s improved as well.\u201d For Wenger, the main difference between the \u00d6zil he signed and the one he will pick this weekend boils down to commitment. \u201cHe is 28, an age where he is more mature. The difference today is he focuses more on efficiency. When he arrived here he was more focused on playing. Today efficiency is more in his mind. Sometimes the trap for very talented players is it is easy for them. They know subconsciously they don\u2019t need to push themselves too hard to be efficient. It\u2019s so easy.\u201d The desire to push himself more is making a tangible difference to his game. \u00d6zil\u2019s increased runs, added to the indefatigable darts of Alexis S\u00e1nchez, make it that much more difficult for defenders to guard their area. Arsenal are less predictable and more punchy. Tottenham arrive at the Emirates as the league\u2019s dominant rearguard, with only five goals conceded, so Arsenal will need their front players to continue their lively form. S\u00e1nchez has scored eight, and \u00d6zil seven (Theo Walcott, who is hoping to return to fitness, also has eight and Olivier Giroud is trying to catch up), so Arsenal feel they have goals in them even against a resolute opponent. So has \u00d6zil changed enough to become the type of player determined to take a tough derby by the scruff of the neck? He excelled recently in the 3-0 defeat of Chelsea, and he will be expected to emulate that performance against Tottenham. \u201cThat\u2019s what you want,\u201d Wenger says. \u201cOf course he can be dangerous but in big games there is always a lot of expectation from him. But I believe that depends more on the team performance. If the team performance is strong then \u00d6zil will always shine.\u201d"

The Rio gold medallist Joanna Rowsell-Shand watched a youthful British quartet of Emily Kay, Emily Nelson, Eleanor Dickinson and Manon Lloyd ride to a composed victory in the 4,000m, along with the \u201cfifth woman\u201d in the Rio squad, Ciara Horne, while another Rio gold winner Katie Archibald has been mentoring the team this weekend. On this showing there is every chance that one or more of the youngsters will be pressing their seniors hard in the run-in to Tokyo. Together with the former sprinter Dani Khan, who rode Thursday\u2019s qualifying round before giving way to Kay, they exude promise and enthusiasm. Nelson and Lloyd are about to celebrate their 20th birthdays \u2013 on Saturday for Lloyd, next weekend for Nelson \u2013 while Kay is relatively senior at 21, and Dickinson is 18, fresh from riding the European Championships a few weeks ago as a junior, and such a new inclusion in the squad she has barely trained with her team mates. The quintet timed their run perfectly for this World Cup. They qualified second fastest on Thursday, then disposed of the Poland quartet in the second round before taking on the Italians \u2013 the fastest qualifiers and the European champions \u2013 in the final. For all their youth they have a wealth of junior and under-23 gold medals at world and European level behind them \u2013 Kay and Nelson won the junior world team pursuit championship here in 2013 \u2013 and they needed that experience in the final, where the Italians were constantly ahead but never by more than around half a second. Lloyd, who had put in a massive lap and three-quarter starting effort to get the team up to speed, paid the price and pulled up with four laps to go. At the same time Italy\u2019s Simone Frapporti swung up for one last time, leaving both teams with the minimum three counters in the final kilometre. It then came down to who had most strength left and when Maria-Giulia Confalioneri slipped off the back of the Italian string with two laps remaining, the crowd sensed a gold medal was imminent if the three British women could keep close to each other\u2019s wheels. By the gun Confalioneri was half a dozen lengths adrift of her team-mates, while the young Britons hung together, inspired by the crowd, according to Kay. \u201cWhen you go into a ride like that and know it\u2019s going to be close, you try to keep to your ride,\u201d she said. \u201cYou can hear the crowd. In a weird way they gave you that extra bit of energy. Down that back straight going into the last lap I had to fight to stay together until the end and I think that made the difference.\u201d Similarly, the Great Britain men\u2019s endurance squad have more than a whiff of the future about them, including a 19-year-old Matt Bostock, a 20-year-old Oliver Wood and a 21-year-old Mark Stewart, who comes from Dundee and the former kilometre rider Kian Emadi-Coffin, who is making the transition from sprinting, plus the seasoned Andy Tennant. Tennant, Wood, Emadi-Coffin and Stewart had to endure a false start in the final against France, who included the Tour de France veteran Sylvain Chavanel along with the promising Corentin Ermenault, who has been linked with Sir Bradley Wiggins\u2019s eponymous team for next season. The British went out fastest and while the French fought back in the second kilometre and ran the home team close throughout the Britons ran out relatively comfortable gold medallists by 1.339sec, but again breaking the 4min barrier to do so. Continuing the theme of youthful promise, the sprinter Ryan Owens, who travelled to Rio as a reserve, qualified second fastest in a promising 9.890sec, and ended up fourth after being taken to three rounds in the quarter-final by his team-mate Joe Truman. At 20 and 19 respectively they have plenty of time ahead and that is something most of the Great Britain team here have in common."
"6||Australia should go to Papua and see the human rights situation for itselfAs Indonesia\u2019s president, Joko \u201cJokowi\u201d Widodo, touches down in Australia for the first time since assuming the presidency, will human rights feature at all in the talks? Indonesian officials are already working overtime to control the agenda, with the defence minister, Ryamizard Ryacudu, warning Australia against \u201cinterfering in the West Papua issue\u201d and asking Australia to send a message to Pacific Island nations that support autonomy for Papuans. Ryamizard told an Australian journalist, with no apparent irony: \u201cThose countries better keep their mouths shut and mind their own business. It is better that Australia speaks to them gently. If it was left up to me, I would twist their ears.\u201d Although Australia has consistently supported Indonesian sovereignty over Papua, the issue remains one of various sensitivities in the Australia\u2013Indonesia relationship. But that discomfort does not give Indonesia a free pass to commit human rights abuses in the province, nor should Australia shy away from discussing such matters at the highest levels. The provinces of Papua and West Papua are a remote, isolated region, where the OPM (Free Papua Movement) has led a low-level insurgency for decades. That insurgency has long been the excuse for significant military involvement in Papua. With the heightened police and military presence, there have been reports of security force abuses including killings, torture, excessive or unnecessary use of force, and mistreatment of peaceful protesters. At least 37 Papuans remain behind bars for peaceful acts of free expression or expressing solidarity with the independence movement. All of this impunity is aided by reduced scrutiny of abuses as foreign journalists and human rights organisations face a half century-long restriction on visiting the province. Perhaps Indonesia feels especially confident in rallying Australian support, since it scored something of a coup in August with the Papua visit by the attorney general, George Brandis. He was the first Australian cabinet minister to visit Papua, and human rights were glaringly absent from all public statements he made about that visit. His choice of travelling companion was also troubling \u2013 he was accompanied by Wiranto, Indonesia\u2019s poster child for impunity for serious abuses. Wiranto, the co-ordinating minister for political, legal and security affairs, was Indonesia\u2019s armed forces chief in 1999 when the military and government-backed militias carried out atrocities against the East Timorese after they voted for independence. In fact, given that background, it\u2019s even more important for the prime minister, Malcolm Turnbull, and foreign minister, Julie Bishop, to ask some hard questions about what Indonesia is doing to address human rights violations in Papua and how Australia can help. Australian acceptance of Indonesian sovereignty over Papua does not mean discussion of human rights concerns in Papua should be taken off the table. Let\u2019s remember that Jokowi himself has previously called for greater respect for human rights in Papua and for the Indonesian government to stop blocking foreign journalists and observers from visiting Papua. Here\u2019s one suggestion that Turnbull could broach with Jokowi: if Indonesian officials are so keen to gain Australia\u2019s support on Papua, then why not allow a multi-party parliamentary delegation from Australia, accompanied by journalists, free access to visit the province? Australia\u2019s parliament has long had an interest in the region, with a Parliamentary Friends of West Papua group co-chaired by Jane Prentice MP and Senator Richard Di Natale. Such a visit, not a junket, should include a range of Australian politicians armed with expertise in trade, tourism, economic and social portfolios and include meetings with Papuan leaders, civil society, imprisoned activists and ordinary Papuans. In fact, when General Luhut Pandjaitan, then coordinating minister for political, legal and security affairs, visited Canberra in June he extended an invitation to the Australian government to visit Papua, saying, I ask also any member of the cabinet to join us to go there. We have nothing to hide. It is not the time to hide something. Let us work together to make it transparent. If you want to criticise Indonesia we are happy to receive that criticism. But please, give that criticism based on data, not rumour. A visit would help Australian politicians understand more about a region only a few hundred kilometres to our north that remains isolated and undeveloped. It would enable Australians to hear directly from Papuans about the issues affecting their lives \u2013 surely this is part of what Jokowi wants in trying to win the hearts and minds of Papuans. Elaine Pearson is the Australia director at Human Rights Watch."

You would lose the mystique of what the Lions are all about.\u201d If anything, Gatland believes the Lions\u2019 tours should be longer, to give the team more time to prepare. Gatland expects about \u201ca third to a half\u201d of his squad will be involved in one or other of the two matches. Gatland made a similar recommendation in his report on the Lions\u2019 successful 2013 tour to Australia, which he felt could be summed up in three words: \u201cpreparation, preparation, preparation\u201d. It is too late to change the 2017 schedule but for 2021 the New Zealander wants the clubs and competitions to agree to structure the season so the Lions have an extra week to get ready, mainly to give the team more time to bond before practice matches. \u201cThe challenge is to get everyone on the same page as quickly as I can, and another week or two would be ideal,\u201d Gatland said. He believes the long lead-up to the first Test, and the tradition of playing midweek matches around the country, are part of the appeal and he added it is the large number of travelling fans that make the Lions so popular in the southern hemisphere. \u201cWatch All Blacks games at Eden Park and 95% of the fans are All Blacks supporters,\u201d Gatland said. \u201cThat\u2019s fine but they\u2019re very excited about going to a ground where there\u2019s red everywhere. \u201cIn the southern hemisphere they never get to experience what we get in the Six Nations, where 40% or more of the crowd are away fans, and that\u2019s what makes the atmosphere.\u201d He hopes the game\u2019s \u201cstakeholders thrash out what\u2019s best, because I would hate to see the Lions die as an entity and not have an opportunity to prepare properly\u201d."

"5||Playboy model charged over Snapchat of naked woman in gym locker roomA Playboy model who used social media to shame a woman at her gym was charged with invasion of privacy by the Los Angeles city prosecutor on Friday, just three months after her Snapchat post mocked the 70-year-old. Earlier this year a California court ruled that a Snapchat secretly taken of a teenager, apparently masturbating in a bathroom stall, was an illegal invasion of privacy. Civil rights attorneys have largely agreed that, under California law, photography or filming without consent in a bathroom is a violation of the \u201creasonable expectation of privacy\u201d. Taking that photograph was \u201ca flagrant violation of privacy law\u201d, explained Danielle Keats Citron, a law professor at the University of Maryland who specializes in privacy and cyber law. \u201cNaked in the bathroom \u2013 it\u2019s uncontested.\u201d"
"7||Why is Australia so obsessed with the espresso martini?When the coffee liqueur brand Mr Black opened sales for the world\u2019s first festival dedicated exclusively to the espresso martini, tickets sold out mere hours. It then added another day to the Melbourne festival \u2013 tickets for which sold out in seven minutes. It then added a third and final day \u2013 which again sold out. Clearly Australian drinkers, particularly Melburnians, are in the depths of a torrid love affair with the espresso martini. There are very few bars in Melbourne that can\u2019t or won\u2019t shake up an espresso martini. Bartender James Armstrong puts it succinctly: \u201cMelbourne loves coffee, Melbourne loves booze. It\u2019s literally the most Melbourne drink since a Melbourne Bitter.\u201d Despite this, the espresso martini is not at all a Melbourne invention \u2013 it was in fact invented in London during the 80s by the late, legendary bartender Dick Bradsell. The story goes that a famous model \u2013 Bradsell, the embodiment of old-school hospitable discretion, never said who \u2013 strode into his bar and asked for a drink that would first \u201cwake me up, then fuck me up\u201d. Bradsell obliged by whipping together the spirit du jour, vodka, some coffee liqueur, some simple syrup, and a shot of freshly pulled espresso. Bradsell christened the drink the \u201cvodka espresso\u201d but it wasn\u2019t long before it was given the name \u201cespresso martini\u201d. Once in Australia \u2013 the Melbourne bartender Fred Siggins suggests that it arrived via UK bartenders on working holiday visas \u2013 the espresso martini found a spiritual home in coffee-loving Melbourne. The bartenders I speak to about its popularity identify several reasons why it took off here. For David Ferris, it\u2019s the perfect common denominator: \u201cThey can fit in any setting: from fancy 40th-floor CBD cocktail bars to grimy rave clubs \u2026 If you have a group of five or more, you can\u2019t get them all to agree on a bitter Italian cocktail or something tequila-based \u2013 this drink works because it appeals to a really wide range of people.\u201d \u201cThe stimulant effect certainly plays a part,\u201d says a bar manager, Graham Espie, \u201ccoupled with Melburnians\u2019 entrenched sensibility to appreciate the bean in every available form.\u201d Venue managers seem to love the drink as much, if not more, than the customers who order it. \u201cIt\u2019s cheap as chips,\u201d Espie says. \u201cIt can cost the venue as little as three dollars, and is sold in some places for over $20.\u201d (Espie adds that at his venue, Howler, \u201cWe keep ours at a reasonable price and use good vodka.\u201d) While some of that mark-up is dedicated to labour costs, there is still some serious coin to be made from them \u2013 especially when compared with other drinks. \u201cI can only sell a vodka Red Bull for $12, when it probably cost me around two dollars and 80 cents to put up,\u201d says Clara Edmonds, \u201cbut I can sell an espresso martini for $20.\u201d The numbers involved are sometimes staggering: Espie estimates that Howler sells 10 times as many espresso martinis as any other cocktail, while Edmonds calculates that at one venue she worked at, more than 50% of the drinks she\u2019d ever made were espresso martinis. It seems that the only people who don\u2019t always feel the love for the drink are bartenders themselves. At a party filled with hospitality workers, I mention I am working on an article about espresso martinis. \u201cDon\u2019t talk to me about fucking espresso martinis!,\u201d one bartender spits. She has, it turns out, recently worked a shift where a hen\u2019s party came in bearing little signs on sticks printed with the words \u201cespresso martini\u201d, so they wouldn\u2019t have to bother talking to their bartender. Another contacts me when she finds out about this article with an impressively vitriolic rant: \u201cEvery arsehole who\u2019s never ordered anything other than Melbourne Bitter and the occasional rum and ginger orders 50 of them at a time under the delusion that it will make them appear to have some kind of class and half an idea about cocktails \u2026 I\u2019ve only met one or two bartenders that don\u2019t sigh or roll their eyes or contemplate suicide every time the words \u2018six expresso martinis thanks, mate\u2019 flop out of some drunkard\u2019s mouth.\u201d Espie argues that familiarity breeds contempt: \u201cMixologists yearn to remain at the cutting edge. If they feel that things are becoming mainstream, they don\u2019t want to be associated with it.\u201d It\u2019s true that, at least to the cocktail cognoscenti, there\u2019s something more than a little daggy about the espresso martini. The name itself is a remnant of the benighted cocktail culture of the 90s, when nearly everything served up in a v-shaped glass was a \u201c-tini\u201d of some sort: apple martini, French martini, flirtini. (Bradsell tried, after the drink had been rechristened as the espresso martini, to rename it the \u201cpharmaceutical stimulant\u201d but it never stuck.) And, as Edmonds notes, it commits another cocktail crime: continuing the somewhat disreputable Prohibition-era trick of disguising the taste of liquor with strong, sweet flavours: \u201cThe espresso martini\u2019s flavour masks the booze \u2013 you can use, excuse my French, shit vodka in an espresso martini and still make it taste good. Particularly in Melbourne, because we have such good coffee.\u201d The flexibility of the cocktail has encouraged experimentation to keep it on-trend, with some Melbourne bars taking it beyond Bradsell\u2019s original four-ingredient recipe. The easiest tweak is a more flavoursome spirit base than the original vodka, such as rum or tequila. More high-end bars use pretty recherch\u00e9 techniques to tart up the drink. Bartender Dominique Cacioli, for instance, developed a version that pairs a chilled base \u2013 Illy espresso liqueur, Chambord, and Woodford Reserve bourbon, blended together and barrel-aged \u2013 with a hot foam flavour, and bitter white chocolate floating on top. Eau-de-Vie Melbourne\u2019s house espresso martini variation similarly comes topped with a liquid nitrogen\u2013chilled sabayon. For professionals in the coffee industry, the espresso martini\u2019s popularity is something of a double-edged sword. Elika Rowell, co-owner of Melbourne coffee roastery Square One, thinks it\u2019s time to reconsider: \u201cChilling espresso over ice shocks it into severe bitterness \u2013 a totally undesirable trait in coffee. Yes, you can add simple syrup to it to try to put a veil over that bitterness, but it will always taste muddy or dirty. Why does everyone want to use a product that actually doesn\u2019t taste good unless you add sugar to it?\u201d Her suggestion? Bars should talk to their coffee suppliers first. \u201cThere are so many awesome coffees around that all taste totally different ... Picking a coffee to suit what you\u2019re doing could be really challenging, but also super-interesting.\u201d According to bartender Clara Edmonds, any drink that wants to take over as the king of caffeinated cocktails would have to jump more than a few hurdles. \u201cPlease, any bartender out there, give me a better option. One that is accessible to everyone, enjoyable for everyone, price-pointed for every owner, easy to make, one that every bartender knows how to make.\u201d If a drink nails all of those key components, Edmonds says, \u201cit will probably take off. But until that better drink comes along, I think the espresso martini will still be Melbourne\u2019s cocktail.\u201d \u2022 The Festival of the Espresso Martini runs until 6 November at the Meat Market, North Melbourne"

"8||Despite the Harvard soccer team decision, men are rarely punished for overt misogynyThe fact that the Harvard men\u2019s soccer team kept a sexual \u201cscouting report\u201d on freshman members of the women\u2019s team is both abhorrent and mostly unsurprising. The only surprise is that such low treatment of women has been documented at all. Most of us, especially at a place like Harvard (I graduated from there in 2013), have learned to avoid making our sexist, racist or otherwise bigoted thoughts so overt by now. We are adept at couching them in traditions and social institutions or confounding them with diversity rhetoric and tokenism. In contrast, this so-called \u201cscouting report\u201d, which the Harvard Crimson reported on last week, is a methodical assessment of the attractiveness of freshman female soccer players. The male soccer players assigned each woman a numerical score, a nickname and a sexual position along with a paragraph weighing her sex appeal. The document was kept on Google Drive and appears to have been amended by members of the men\u2019s soccer team each year since it started in 2012. Its release amounts to the outing of a group of men so confident in the unassailability of their privilege as to revel in it, record it and build a team culture around it. Harvard College made an attempt to rectify institutionalized inequality in last year\u2019s decision to bar members of exclusive \u201cfinal clubs\u201d \u2013 single-sex, exclusive social clubs \u2013 from occupying leadership roles in school-sanctioned clubs or athletics and from receiving top academic scholarships like Rhodes or Fulbright. The move was a broad-stroke attempt to dismantle what the dean of the college, Rakesh Khurana, called, in a public letter \u201cthe perpetuation of spaces that are rife with power imbalances\u201d. Such power imbalances, of course, are the context in which a 2015 survey found that 31% of Harvard senior undergraduate females had experienced sexual assault during their time on campus. They are also the context in which Harvard is fighting a Title IX lawsuit by a recent graduate accusing the school of \u201cdeliberate indifference\u201d to her case after she reported being sexually abused by another student. The cancellation of the remainder of the men\u2019s soccer team\u2019s season, while fair, is insufficient punishment in the larger context of Harvard\u2019s entrenched misogyny problem. The season was already close to its end, so the punishment amounts to a relatively minor rebuke for the many male soccer players who have contributed to this codified team activity of demeaning their female counterparts over the last four years. It\u2019s been a big year for watching sexual harassment charges bounce off of highly privileged white men. Donald Trump initially suffered in polling after the incriminating Access Hollywood tape was released; Stanford swimmer Brock Turner was subject to criminal charges and internet vitriol after he was accused of rape; and these Harvard soccer players have lost the chance to compete in the NCAA tournament this season. But then the gap in the presidential polls slimmed, Turner was sentenced to only six months in jail, and the soccer players just have a little more free time over the next few weeks than they expected. This should not be the end of it. This imperviousness often leaves women, people of color and other oppressed groups trying to pin down the plausibly deniable injustices that imbue their lives. This has fed the recent student demands for safer spaces and higher cultural sensitivity at colleges and universities throughout the country, most notably at Yale and Oberlin. We try to police micro-aggressions and cultural appropriation because there are often not more substantial things to grab on to and eradicate. And when there are, we apparently hesitate to punish the patriarchy being enacted before our eyes. We should not have to wait for events like the release of this despicable soccer document to expect more systemic and sustainable steps, both by administrations to support students and by these male athletes and others to set a standard of real respect for their female peers. These soccer players should be required to demonstrate genuine contrition and to attempt real reform in order to deserve their spots on a team that represents Harvard College. (Ed note: they issued an apology in the Harvard Crimson after this column was originally published.) Hopefully this will force even male teams or clubs that don\u2019t maintain written assessments of women\u2019s attractiveness to examine the ways they participate in power imbalances. Harvard\u2019s response is a good start, but it\u2019s only that."
"2||Pinochet's torture ship sails into Sydney, sparking protestsThe appearance of a Chilean naval vessel in Sydney Harbour, which had been used as a \u201ctorture chamber\u201d by the Pinochet regime, has sparked protests from Chilean-Australians who say it should be removed from service. The Esmeralda, a four-masted tall ship that is nearly 400ft long, is in Sydney as part of its 61st training cruise and has been docked at Garden Island since Thursday morning. Considered a national symbol of Chile, it is used as a sail training vessel by the country\u2019s navy and spends about half the year sailing around the world. It was last in Sydney in 2012. But its tours have sparked controversy after a series of human rights reports revealed that the ship was used to detain victims of the Augusto Pinochet\u2019s regime in September 1973. A 1986 US Senate report suggesting that as many as 112 people \u2013 including 40 women \u2013 were held on the ship, with rape, electric shocks, mock executions and beatings among the crimes reportedly carried out on board. A British-Chilean priest, Father Michael Woodward, who had been tortured on board, was taken off the vessel for treatment at a naval hospital but died of his injuries in transit. In 2003 Amnesty International urged Chile\u2019s government and navy to acknowledge serious human rights violations committed on the boat. In light of its bloody history, its continued use has prompted quayside protests around the world. At the weekend the Esmeralda will be docked at the Sydney fleet base on Cowper Wharf Road in Woolloomooloo and open to the public for guided tours. The Chile Solidarity Committee, a Sydney-based organisation of about 50 people, intends to protest against the vessel\u2019s appearance on Saturday. An organiser, Vlaudin Vega, said the Esmeralda had been used as a \u201ctorture chamber\u201d in 1973 but the Chilean navy had never acknowledged that chapter in the ship\u2019s history. \u201cWe need to tell the world, the Australian people, that in this vessel crimes were committed and the navy needs to respond.\u201d More than 330 crew are onboard the ship. Vega suggested that, because of their age, many would be unaware of its history. He said removing the ship from service would be a symbolic gesture that would help the Chilean community to \u201cheal those wounds\u201d of Pinochet\u2019s regime, though that might require a \u201cmore radical government\u201d in Chile. \u201cRegardless of what the present purpose of the ship is, crimes were committed there,\u201d he said. \u201cPeople got killed there. \u201c[The navy needs] to take into account what happened to the most prestigious ship that they have \u2026 There\u2019s no question it\u2019s a beautiful ship but they need to retire it as a symbol of some accountability.\u201d The Chilean-Australian Association, the consulate general of Chile in Sydney and the embassy of Chile in Australia \u2013 which have promoted the Esmeralda\u2019s appearance in Sydney \u2013 have been contacted for comment. The ship is due to depart on Monday afternoon and will return to Chile next month."
"2||Norfolk Island body issuing Australian gambling licences to be shut downThe Norfolk Island Gaming Authority will be shut down after a scathing report found it to be \u201cbarely viable\u201d, failing in its regulatory role, and with internal controls that could \u201cgive rise to fraud and corruption\u201d. The report, based on a two-month investigation of the authority and the Norfolk Island administration, found a litany of serious concerns including a lack of transparency, oversight, or resources, a disregard for risks or appropriate regulation, and serious concerns over conflicts of interest in appointments. \u201cThe authority and the former administration have been more concerned about raising revenue from gaming licences than having due regard to its regulatory functions,\u201d the report said. \u201cWe recommend that it not continue to operate in its current form.\u201d The gaming authority of the Pacific island \u2013 an Australian territory \u2013 was reviewed in April and May of this year under instruction by the Norfolk Island administration, after concerns were raised about its practises. The then federal minister for territories, Paul Fletcher, revoked the authority\u2019s power to license betting agencies in April following revelations it had effectively given permission for the world\u2019s largest illegal betting organisation to operate. The report, released by the federal government on Saturday, came to the overall conclusion that the gaming authority was \u201clargely ineffective\u201d and had \u201cfailed to fulfil its regulatory role to an acceptable level\u201d. The federal minister for local government and territories, Fiona Nash said there was no choice but to shut down the authority based on the final report, and an earlier draft, by the assessor, Centium. \u201cGambling in Australia must be carefully regulated to ensure the integrity of our sport and to protect consumers,\u201d Nash said. \u201cBefore arriving at this decision, I asked Centium to undertake a further assessment to determine whether it would be possible to rectify the myriad of issues identified in the first report. \u201cCentium\u2019s report made it abundantly clear that the authority is beyond redemption and that these problems cannot be resolved satisfactorily. As a result, I am entirely confident that closing the authority is the right thing to do.\u201d The report found the authority was \u201clargely ineffective\u201d in regards to two thirds of Centium\u2019s terms of reference, and \u201cpartially ineffective\u201d for the rest. It found the authority was \u201cgrossly under-resourced\u201d. \u201cBasic control elements are not in place, such as: governance and reporting structures, a risk register, contracts with key personnel, segregation of duties, controls to prevent conflicts of interest, staff remuneration processes and policies and procedures.\u201d It found the internal control system was lacking, leading to a perception that it was \u201chighly susceptible to fraud and corruption\u201d. The investigation found the director of the authority had not formally disclosed he was the brother-in-law of the former Norfolk Island chief minister and minister for tourism, industry and development, and concerns had been raised about the latter\u2019s influence on internal audits. The appointment of a lottery agency\u2019s chief operating officer as a contractor to provide regulatory services was also a \u201cserious error of judgement\u201d by the authority and its director, who the report said was aware of the contractor\u2019s position. \u201cThere is a clear conflict of interest between the authority and a person it regulates,\u201d it said. \u201cThe board should have been advised of this conflict. Importantly, none of the licensees interviewed by us were aware of his senior position with a competing licensee.\u201d Nash said current betting agencies have until March 2017 to find licenses in other jurisdictions, and the federal government will cover the \u201csmall shortfall\u201d in revenue for Norfolk Island. From July Norfolk Island lost the right to self-govern after it was brought under Australian tax and welfare systems, an act which caused split among the island\u2019s community."
"6||Ominous news for Aleppo as Russian frigate reaches Syrian coastA Russian frigate armed with cruise missiles has passed through the Bosphorus on its way to the eastern Mediterranean in a potentially ominous development for the embattled Syrian city of Aleppo. The Admiral Grigorovich, part of Russia\u2019s Black Sea fleet, arrived off the Syrian coast on Friday as the latest pause in the Russian bombardment of eastern Aleppo came to an end, adding to an emphatic Russian show of naval force in the Mediterranean. Unlike the Admiral Kuznetsov aircraft carrier and Peter the Great nuclear-powered battle cruiser, whose arrival in the region has drawn considerable publicity, the newly commissioned Grigorovich has a fearsome ground attack capability in the form of Kalibr land-attack cruise missiles. Three Russian submarines from Russia\u2019s northern fleet capable of firing Kalibr missiles are also reported to have arrived in the Mediterranean. Russia has used Kalibr missiles, equivalent to US Tomahawk missiles, against Syrian targets a handful of times over the past year. The concentration of forces in the eastern Mediterranean in support of the Syrian regime\u2019s ambitions to retake Aleppo from rebel forces, suggest they may be used again in the coming few days or hours. Earlier this week, Vladimir Putin warned Syrian rebels and civilians trapped in eastern Aleppo to leave the city by Friday night, when a temporary moratorium on air strikes was set to expire. But opposition groups told the Guardian that promised safe passages out of besieged areas did not exist. As the deadline drew near, opposition groups pushed on with an assault on loyalist west Aleppo, while residents seemed resigned to a resumption in airstrikes. \u201cNothing can be done. Nobody can stop the planes,\u201d Bebars Mishal, an official with the White Helmets volunteer rescue service, told Reuters. The Kuznetsov battle group paused in its approach towards Syria off the east coast of Crete on Thursday, to carry out aviation exercises. Russian ministry of defence footage showed warplanes taking off and landing on its deck. The Kuznetsov is carrying about 10 Sukhoi-33 and four Mig-29 fighter aircraft, as well as up to two dozen helicopters. Although the Su-33s have recently been adapted to drop bombs more accurately, the plane has never been used for ground attack. Only the Mig-29 is designed for that purpose. If the planes are used to bomb targets in Syria, it would mark the first time Russia has used its only aircraft carrier in combat. That lack of experience, however, may limit its usefulness. \u201cThere are very few carrier-trained pilots. In fact, there are more planes than pilots, and most of the planes on board are not made for ground attack. For naval aviation this is largely a training run,\u201d Michael Kofman, a Russian specialist at the Centre for Naval Analyses, a federally funded research institute. \u201cThey haven\u2019t done anything done anything like this in a long time, and its getting heavily covered by the local and international media,\u201d said Peter Zwack, a retired US brigadier general and former military attache to Moscow, now at the National Defence University. \u201cThey are arriving in the eastern Mediterranean right before our election. It\u2019s posturing and secondarily adding capability in Syria.\u201d The visit of its flagship to the Mediterranean is also a morale-boosting demonstration of military capacity for the Russian navy, which has not played a significant role in the Ukraine or Syrian conflicts, but which is seen by Vladimir Putin as vital to Russian self-image as a global power. \u201cRussia\u2019s naval presence in the Mediterranean is as much about military posture and ability to project power beyond its region as much as the Syria campaign in general. Moscow sees it as important attributes of a \u2018great power\u2019,\u201d said Maxim Suchkov, an expert on the Russian Foreign Affairs Council and an editor at Al-Monitor covering Russian relations with the Middle East. The importance of image to this naval expedition is illustrated by the fact that Russian sailors have spent several days during its Mediterranean journey painting its deck bright blue, presumably so it looks better in aerial photographs. The carrier has had a long and troubled history. It was launched in 1985, but took a decade to become fully operational. It was first called the Riga, then renamed the Leonid Brezhnev as the former Soviet premier died, and then the Tbilisi, after the Georgian capital, and then rechristened after the Soviet Union fell apart and Georgia gained independence. It was initially armed with a dozen Granit anti-ship missiles, but those have recently been removed to make more space for aircraft. The carrier has had persistent problems with its propulsion system, based on turbo-pressurised boilers, which belch out black smoke due to an excess of fuel over air in its engines. They have frequently broken down and require the Kuznetsov to be accompanied on high seas duty by a tug. The ship has spent more time in port than at sea and will be out of action for several years for refurbishment from next year. It is accompanied by the Peter the Great, a massive battle cruiser, with a fearsome array of missiles, but which are designed to hit planes, ships and submarines rather than attack land targets. A more potent threat from the flotilla sent from Russia\u2019s Northern Fleet may have escorted the Kuznetsov under the surface. UK reports, quoting British government sources, say two nuclear-powered Akula-class and one diesel-powered Kilo-class submarine have been spotted trailing the battle group into the Mediterranean. At the same time as the Kuznetsov left Murmansk, two destroyers left Vladivostok, the headquarters of the Pacific Fleet. Those destroyers, the Bystry and the Admiral Tributs, are reported to be off the Gulf of Aden. Kofman said that they could be on call in case the Kuznetsov encounters engine trouble, \u201cpossibly a Plan B for nervous admirals\u201d. However, the Bystry, has the same unreliable propulsion system. The arrival of the Grigorovich and the Russian submarines armed with cruise missiles could add to the devastation being wreaked on eastern Aleppo, home to 275,000 people. However, if that was Russia\u2019s only aim, it could easily increase the number of planes it bases at Hmeimim air base, near Latakia. The flexing of naval muscle is about geopolitical image as much as military firepower, but it carries with it the risk of humiliation. The Russian naval base at Tartus is not equipped to host large ships like the Kuznetsov and there are no friendly naval bases nearby in case of breakdown. \u201cIt\u2019s a dice roll,\u201d Kofman said. \u201cIf anything happens it will be a long tow back home.\u201d \u2022 The subheading of this article was amended on 8 November 2016 to correct a misspelling of Admiral Grigorovich."
"2||Public figures call on government to hold free vote on same-sex marriageA group of 114 allies of the LGBTI community have signed a statement calling for the government to ditch the same-sex marriage plebiscite and hold a free vote on marriage equality.
