"23||The Witches: Salem, 1692, A History review \u2013 what drove the puritans to such hysteria?Translucent cats, balls of fire, yellow birds, spectral women perched on roof beams: Salem\u2019s puritans lived in a dull, damp, muddy-hemmed world, but as Stacy Schiff\u2019s account of the most infamous of all witch trials shows, the creatures they imagined in the heat of the hysteria that gripped them during 1692 offered lurid compensation. Fourteen women and five men were executed for witchcraft during that terrible Massachusetts year, just a fraction of those accused of pinching and poking a chorus of convulsing \u201cvisionary\u201d adolescent girls, or of suckling demons, or appearing by the beds of God-fearing men and women. Burrowing deep into her sources, Schiff enters the world of accusers and accused, finding the humanity and horror in the grim gothic landscape of meeting house, parsonage and gallows. Her psychological insights are compelling, exploring how the fearful repressions of a Puritan childhood and the pressures of small-town isolation could have led to such extreme events. She is firstly a masterful storyteller, though, bringing these characters, her own cast of witch\u2019s poppets out of their 17th-century gloom and into chattering, shaking, finger-pointing life. \u2022 The Witches is published by Weidenfeld &amp; Nicolson. To order a copy for \u00a38.19 (RRP \u00a39.99) go to bookshop.theguardian.com or call 0330 333 6846. Free UK p&amp;p over \u00a310, online orders only. Phone orders min p&amp;p of \u00a31.99."
"4||Rochdale\u2019s Calvin Andrew handed 12-match ban for violent conductThe Rochdale striker Calvin Andrew has been banned for 12 matches after an incident against Oldham \u2013 one of the longest suspensions in Football League history. The Football Association said the former Luton, Crystal Palace and Port Vale player \u201cwas involved in an act of violent conduct in or around the 77th minute of the game\u201d, which Rochdale won 1-0. It is believed the incident concerned the Oldham defender Peter Clarke, who was struck by an elbow near the edge of his six-yard box as a Rochdale attack broke down close to the corner flag. The FA\u2019s statement said the incident was not seen by the match officials but caught on video: \u201cIt was alleged the standard punishment of three matches that would otherwise apply would be clearly insufficient. The player admitted his behaviour constituted violent conduct but denied the clearly insufficient element of the charge. However, this was found proven following an Independent Regulatory Commission hearing.\u201d Andrew, who signed a new one-year contract in the summer having moved to Rochdale in 2014, will not play again this year. Only Eric Cantona has been given a more hefty sentence after the Manchester United forward was banned for nine months in 1995 for striking a Crystal Palace supporter. Joey Barton was also banned for 12 matches in 2012 for two charges of violent conduct in QPR\u2019s 3-2 defeat at Manchester City."
"17||M&S expected to announce closure of up to 30 storesMarks &amp; Spencer is expected to announce the closure of up to 30 stores next week as part of an attempt to revive the British retailer. Smaller outlets in flagging towns and shopping centres are likely to form the bulk of the closures, and other stores could be relocated or downsized over several years, potentially leading to thousands of job losses. Shopfloor space is expected to be reallocated, as the Guardian first reported earlier this year, meaning bigger food halls and smaller clothing areas. Unwanted floorspace could be given over to other businesses including retailers and restaurants. Sources told Sky News, which first reported the closures, that they would number \u201cin the low dozens\u201d. Like others on the high street, M&amp;S is struggling to adapt to changing shopping habits. As more people make purchases online, smaller or less attractive town centres and shopping centres are losing out. Clothing retailers have had a difficult year of unseasonal weather, while shoppers have been spending more on eating out, holidays and technology, and less on fashion. The M&amp;S chief executive, Steve Rowe, is expected to announce the store cuts as part of a wide strategic rethink. He is also expected to close stores in China, where M&amp;S has found trading tough, and signal further shutdowns in continental Europe. Rowe is expected to focus instead on expanding M&amp;S\u2019s successful food business, which plans to add 200 small shops by 2019. The latest move comes after M&amp;S announced plans to cut 525 London head office jobs as part of Rowe\u2019s attempts to reduce costs and simplify the business. He is attempting to turn around M&amp;S after the biggest fall in its clothing sales since the 2008 financial crisis. Last year M&amp;S closed a net five \u201cdiscount\u201d stores and one high street outlet in the UK, but increased total trading space slightly by opening dozens of franchised Simply Food stores. The group closed 13 stores in Europe last year. M&amp;S has about 300 full-range stores, 640 food-only outlets, 131 of which are larger food halls, and more than 400 stores overseas. Tony Shiret, an analyst at Haitong, said M&amp;S probably needed to close more than 30 stores, given the changing nature of shopping and years of poor performance by the retailer\u2019s clothing ranges. \u201cThere are a lot of traditional high street shops that have not had a lot of money spent on them for a long time,\u201d he said, suggesting that M&amp;S should shut 20% of its space, as many as 60 stores. But he added: \u201cIt\u2019s not that easy to close that amount of space and when you do, it puts pressures on your supply chain.\u201d"

"17||Housing associations added to Bank's bond-buying schemeThe Bank of England has added housing associations to its \u00a310bn corporate bond-buying scheme for the first time, which could give a boost to housebuilding by reducing the cost of borrowing. The move comes after lobbying from the National Housing Federation and and other groups such as Positive Money, and Theresa May\u2019s criticism of the Bank\u2019s quantitative easing programme and its \u201cbad side-effects\u201d for poorer people. The Bank\u2019s governor, Mark Carney, had said companies will have to show they make a \u201cmaterial contribution to the UK economy\u201d to qualify for the corporate bond programme. There was some surprise, however, when the list of bonds included foreign-owned corporations with limited UK operations, such as Apple, rather than businesses that bolster the UK\u2019s infrastructure. Buying the bonds of housing associations could make borrowing cheaper, reducing interest payments and increasing the amount of money available to invest in housing. However, housing experts questioned whether investors such as pension funds would want to buy those bonds, as other bonds currently offer better returns. Peabody, one of the UK\u2019s oldest housing associations, said it was delighted by the Bank\u2019s move, and that as a result its cost of borrowing had fallen by eight basis points. Peabody owns assets worth \u00a33bn and plans to build 8,000 homes. It is leading a \u00a31.5bn regeneration project in Thamesmead, south-east London, which is expected to deliver up to 20,000 homes. It includes the revamp of a 1970s housing estate that served as the backdrop to A Clockwork Orange. Susan Hickey, Peabody\u2019s chief finance officer, said: \u201cThis is a historic achievement for the sector, demonstrating real confidence in our ability, in our financial strength and capacity to help tackle the housing crisis. This should make it cheaper to borrow and make it easier for us to invest and attract investment in new homes, communities and infrastructure. It is great news.\u201d The editor of Social Housing magazine, Luke Cross, said: \u201cThis obviously could accelerate housing and regeneration schemes, like Peabody\u2019s in Thamesmead.\u201d But he added a note of caution: \u201cOne question is whether investors like pension funds will want to sell these bonds. They still need to find somewhere safe to invest people\u2019s pensions, and many of the bonds they have at the moment offer better returns than they could get in the market today.\u201d The amount of bonds bought by the Bank of England will depend on the size of housing associations\u2019 issuance. Property and finance made up 2% of the Bank\u2019s corporate bond purchase programme in September."

"22||Scottish ministers ban police from searching children for alcoholScottish ministers have banned police from stopping and searching children for alcohol without legal cause after they found little evidence to support its use. To the irritation of some rank and file officers, Michael Matheson, the Scottish justice secretary, said the government would instead focus on introducing a new statutory code of practice to cover searches of adults next year. \u201cWe know stop and search can be a valuable tool on combating crime, but it is important that we get the balance right between protecting the public and the rights of individuals,\u201d Matheson said. Scottish police will still have the legal power to require an underage child to hand over alcohol if they can see it on them but, after this new bar on non-statutory searches, would need to arrest them if they refuse to hand it over. The decision follows lengthy reviews into the once heavy use of non-statutory stop and searches in Scotland including children younger than 10, which became particularly widespread in Strathclyde under the then chief constable Sir Stephen House. Unlike in England and Wales, where the practice is heavily regulated, Scottish police were repeatedly using common law powers for \u201cconsensual\u201d searches of children without oversight and legal cause. At its peak, Scotland\u2019s overall stop and search rate was four times higher than in England and Wales. A study by academic researcher Kath Murray, with the Scottish Centre for Crime and Justice Research, found under-14s were searched 26,000 times in 2010 without the specific statutory power to do so. That included 500 searches of children under-10s. In Strathclyde, there were more recorded searches of 16-year-olds than the number of people that age living in the force area, at a rate of 1,406 searches per 1,000 people. The police and the then justice secretary, Kenny MacAskill, insisted that non-statutory stop and searches were an essential weapon in the wider strategy of combating underage drinking and antisocial behaviour. Few children objected and many adults supported it, they said. In 2015, the UN human rights committee said the practice was \u201callegedly unlawful and disproportionate\u201d and should be scrapped. But an expert panel set up by Scottish ministers to review all stop and search policies could not agree on whether to do so or not, leading to the latest review. However, the Scottish government\u2019s review, published on Friday, found that after stricter oversight was introduced in the summer of 2015, there were still few successful discoveries of alcohol on children. Of 1,629 consensual searches of children, only 158 \u2013 or less than one search in 10 \u2013 found alcohol. For searches involving adults aged 18 or over, the figure reached 16.3%. There were far more cases \u2013 2,551 in total \u2013 where children voluntarily handed over alcohol without being searched. Matheson said the question of non-statutory search powers of children would be revisited 12 months after a new code of conduct comes into force next year, to see if there was clear evidence the power was needed. Murray said this was a measured and welcome decision by ministers. \u201cAny increase in the powers of the police should be evidence based, and as the Scottish government and Police Scotland have acknowledged, there is currently insufficient evidence to support such a move.\u201d The Scottish Police Federation, which represents rank and file officers, would not comment directly on the government\u2019s decision. Calum Steele, its general secretary, tweeted:"
"6||As Nicaragua's first couple consolidates power, a daughter fears for her countryMost daughters would be proud of a mother on the verge of a historic election victory, but the prospect brings pain and dread to Zoilam\u00e9rica Narv\u00e1ez Murillo, who says she fears the politics of her nation are becoming as destructively incestuous as that of her family. Narv\u00e1ez is the outcast child of Nicaragua\u2019s first lady, Rosario Murillo, who is standing for the first time in this Sunday\u2019s election on the same ticket as her husband, the veteran Sandinista leader and national president Daniel Ortega. Polls suggest the first couple will win by a landslide, prompting comparisons with the Underwoods in the TV drama House of Cards, outrage among an opposition who claim a rigged poll is cementing the power of an authoritarian dynasty, and despondency in their daughter. Narv\u00e1ez claims Ortega, her stepfather, sexually abused her from the age of nine years old. When she went public with these accusations in 1998, her mother turned against her and led a campaign of denials that propelled the president\u2019s wife into a more powerful position both in her marital relationship and national government. In the years since, Murillo has moved from the fringes to the centre of Nicaraguan politics. She is already de facto chief of staff, the main spokesman of the administration and a micromanager whose approval is needed for every ministerial interview or policy draft. The vice-presidency will formalise her power. According to Narv\u00e1ez, it will also complete a cynical trade-off. \u201cI feel like I was sold, that I was part of a transaction. Since I came out and revealed what happened, she has tried to eliminate the truth. It\u2019s part of an agreement she has with Daniel. She told him, \u2018Don\u2019t worry, I\u2019ll deal with it\u2019,\u201d Narv\u00e1ez told the Guardian. \u201cShe denied the sexual abuse of her daughter to prove her loyalty and maintain power. It was politics that motivated her.\u201d Ortega and Murillo did not respond to requests for an interview, but they have previously denied Narv\u00e1ez\u2019s accusations and argued that political opponents are trying to use them to undermine a popular government. They are by no means the only allegations against the first couple, who have also alienated many former associates with their increasingly authoritarian leadership. Ortega is accused of using his influence over the courts and the electoral council to overcome constitutional term limits for himself and to block the opposition alliance that came second in the last election \u2013 headed by the Independent Liberal party \u2013 from fielding their chosen candidates. Despite concerns about previous elections in 2008 and 2011, the president has ignored reform recommendations by monitors from the European Union and Organisation of American States (OAS). \u201cThis is the moment of total collapse for the Nicaraguan democratic system. In the past 40 years, we have not seen an election that is less credible or more fraudulent,\u201d said Dora Mar\u00eda T\u00e9llez, founder of the breakaway Sandinista Renovation Movement, which was earlier stripped of its legal status. \u201cOrtega controls the entire process \u2013 who can contest, who monitors the vote and how the count is organised.\u201d Like Ortega, T\u00e9llez risked her life for the Sandinista revolution. In 1978, she was among the leaders of a daring mission that seized the national assembly and took 1,500 people hostage, including most of the congressmen. This was a turning point in the struggle against the dictatorship of Anastasio Somoza Debayle and she hoped it would usher in a period of greater freedoms and social justice. But under Ortega, she says the country appears to be going backwards to an old system of a family dictatorship. T\u00e9llez said that as well as nominating his wife as vice-president, Ortega has ensured the first couple\u2019s children have dominant positions in the tourist industry, the media, and business with China (including over the currently stalled plans for a canal), while their in-laws run the police and oil distribution networks. \u201cThe family of Ortega and Murillo have taken over the Sandinista National Liberation Front (FSLN) like a parasite, using their control to benefit their family. It is basically the same as what the Somozas did,\u201d she told the Guardian, as a tropical rainstorm lashed the streets of Managua, the country\u2019s capital. \u201cHe\u2019s a representative of big capital. He and his family are now among the richest in Nicaragua. This is a profoundly corrupt system that completely betrays the principles of the Sandinista movement.\u201d But many voters remain loyal to the FSLN, which introduced free healthcare and education and more recent benefits \u2013 such as free Wi-Fi in city parks \u2013 that other governments failed to deliver to one of the poorest countries in Latin America. With decades of experience, the first couple have also proved among the wiliest political operators in the region, building alliances with key sectors of society, pursuing business-friendly policies, and ruthlessly applying a strategy of divide and rule for their enemies, all the while ensuring an ever tighter grip on all branches of government and the election process. The powerful Catholic and evangelical lobbies are happy the government has committed itself to strict anti-abortion laws, devoted public funds to church renovation, and that the first couple are running under a slogan of \u201cChristian, socialist, solidarity\u201d \u2013 which can be seen on pink and purple roadside banners across the capital. Corporations like the government\u2019s orthodox macroeconomic policy, relatively low crime rates compared with most other central American neighbours, and a GDP growth rate that has been ticking along at about 4.5% for the past two years, despite the sharp decline in financial support from Venezuela and a fall in commodity prices. The result is some way from socialism or democracy, but it has proved resilient. While other leftist governments in Latin America have been pushed from power, lost key elections or come under pressure in the past year, Ortega and Murillo remain far ahead in the polls. With the opposition denied or divided, there is no real contest so the strongest vote against the government is likely to come from the campaign for abstentions and deliberately spoiled ballots. It will be hard to know how effective this proves, however, because the result will be easy to manipulate without independent monitors. Concerns about the vote may lead to repercussions. The OAS has said it will issue a report on the electoral system six months from now. The United States Senate is also debating a bill \u2013 the Nicaragua Investment Conditionality Act (more widely known simply as NICA) \u2013 that would impose financial controls on the country if it restricts multi-party democracy. But any punishment would come too late to affect the outcome, which means Ortega is almost certain to secure his third consecutive term, with Murillo becoming his vice-president. How much further she can go is now the subject of speculation, fired by unsubstantiated rumours her husband is unwell. Some gossip that the old guard of the Sandinistas are manoeuvring to block her from taking over if Ortega died. Others believe Murillo \u2013 a poet \u2013 is already in the driving seat, due to her increasingly prominent role. It is her voice that updates the nation with a radio broadcast each lunchtime. It is her artistic tastes that have resulted in Managua\u2019s roadsides being adorned with numerous luridly coloured steel \u201ctrees of life\u201d. \u201cI think she is more powerful than him,\u201d said Maria Fernanda Flores, a Liberal opposition congressional candidate and wife of the former president Arnoldo Alem\u00e1n. \u201cShe\u2019s the only one who talks. You don\u2019t hear other ministers. You almost never hear Daniel. You forget what his voice is like.\u201d Narv\u00e1ez, however, remembers. She said she was driven to exile in Costa Rica by the harassment she faced after her accusations, and that she cannot forget the violence in her family and the lies that followed so they could maintain power. She warned the country could be in for something similar. \u201cThe abuser of a child captures, isolates and brainwashes their victim. That is what is happening to Nicaragua, which has a political system controlled by one family that denies opposition,\u201d she said. This article was corrected on 5 November 2016. The Sandinista National Liberation Front is referred to as the FSLN, not the FLSN."
"17||Wetherspoon boss Tim Martin: \u2018The EU became a quasi-religion, and universities are the modern-day seminaries\u2019Every time I interview a Brexiteer, I come away more confused than I arrived. This week I thought I was going to meet the pub trade\u2019s real life Al Murray, for the parallels between Tim Martin\u2019s public image and the fictional Eurosceptic landlord appear disconcertingly close. In fact, on the train to JD Wetherspoon HQ in Watford I opened the Guardian to find a quiz inviting readers to guess which quotes to attribute to each publican. When I run the quiz past Martin later, even he gets two answers wrong. The founder of Wetherspoon\u2019s first entered the debate about Europe in 2002, when he distributed 500,000 beer mats across his pub chain, urging Britain not to join the euro. Before this year\u2019s referendum he printed up another half a million mats, encouraging his customers to back Brexit. One of Vote Leave\u2019s few high-profile businessmen, the multi-millionaire was back in the headlines this week, attacking EU leaders for \u201chectoring\u201d the UK. If Jean-Claude Juncker doesn\u2019t stop \u201cbullying\u201d us, he warned, Wetherspoon pubs might stop stocking European wines and lagers and buy its drinks elsewhere. \u201cBritons could BOYCOTT European booze if Juncker punishes UK, warns Wetherspoons boss,\u201d whooped a Daily Express headline. But Martin\u2019s vision of Brexit turns out to be very different to that of others on his side. During the referendum campaign, a diversity of reasons for leaving was one of the leave side\u2019s great strengths; amid the chorus of different arguments there was something for almost everyone. Now that they have won, however, their incompatibility is becoming overwhelmingly clear. In many respects, Martin\u2019s view of Europe is as unlike Nigel Farage\u2019s \u2013 or Al Murray\u2019s \u2013 as mine. We meet in his office, an unostentatious room cluttered with boxes of Mo\u00ebt champagne, which he gives to office staff when it\u2019s their birthday. At 6ft 6in, well built and ruddy-faced, he looks more like a farmer than a multi-millionaire chairman, and now that he has tamed his silver mullet into a more conventional haircut, the most striking thing about him is his voice. Martin grew up in Northern Ireland and New Zealand, and the accent meanders between the two, via the West Country, interrupted by endless eruptions of booming laughter. I can\u2019t remember meeting anyone who laughs so much, so I ask if he is enjoying his role as a political campaigner. \u201cWell, it\u2019s quite weird, actually,\u201d he admits, \u201cbecause it was unplanned.\u201d In 2002, in the wake of the ERM debacle and with the prospect of Britain joining the euro, Martin made the \u201cbanal observation\u201d that \u201cevery other currency in the world has got a government. The euro can\u2019t work without a government. I kept waiting for a Harvard professor to come out of the woodwork and say, \u2018I\u2019ve never heard such ignorance in my life.\u2019 But they never did. So I just kept repeating it. I became someone on the national news, repeating the same phrase.\u201d He breaks into a cackle. \u201cAnd in the end, the point was true.\u201d Once Britain opted not to join the euro, Martin lost interest in Europe. \u201cI never said a word.\u201d Had we met even just a couple of years ago, would he even have described himself as fiercely Eurosceptic? \u201cNot in the way that a lot of fiercely Eurosceptic people were. I think I was probably just starting to become aware of the lack of democracy at the heart of the EU, and formed the amateur philosophical theory that for the future of humanity \u2013 for prosperity and peace \u2013 you must have democracy. Because if you don\u2019t have democracy,\u201d and he starts to laugh again, \u201cyou always end up with a nutter in charge.\u201d (Does the same apply to businesses? \u201cYes! Even more so, only the customers are the electorate.\u201d) Martin wouldn\u2019t even have been too unhappy for us to remain in the EU. \u201cI just agreed with David Cameron that it needed fundamental reform.\u201d The 61-year-old is of the firm, if increasingly heretical, opinion that Britain has benefited enormously from EU migration, \u201cnot just economically but socially and culturally\u201d. His preferred Brexit model would continue to allow anyone from current EU member states to live and work here. \u201cIf you look around the world, successful economies and countries have had gradually rising populations, and that\u2019s needed for the UK as well.\u201d The only change he would make to the free movement of labour would be to exclude countries who join the EU in the future. How, then, did he feel when the leave campaign focused so heavily on immigration? He exhales uneasily, ums and ahs, and says: \u201cWell, you\u2019ll always get an element of xenophobia in any society, but I don\u2019t think Britain is generally xenophobic. There was one poster I didn\u2019t think should have been put there. But for me, I argued from the point of democracy.\u201d Martin doesn\u2019t even share most Brexiteers\u2019 fury at the high court ruling requiring parliament\u2019s consent to trigger article 50. \u201cWell, it creates a bit more uncertainty,\u201d he offers, \u201cso I can\u2019t exactly welcome it. But I\u2019ll support the legal outcome, because I believe in the law, and I believe in parliament, and I believe in democracy.\u201d The democratic deficit is Martin\u2019s sole objection to the EU project. \u201cThe thing I cannot understand is that when I said I think democracy is crucial for the future of the world, I\u2019d have thought people would pour out of the doors saying, \u2018Yes! Tim is right!\u2019 But people on the remain side just don\u2019t seem to think like that. The emotion of people who\u2019ve been to good universities, et cetera, is not that they owe everything to democracy, but that they owe everything to people like them, who are better able to run companies, the civil service, universities, societies in general.\u201d The phrase \u201cbetter able to run companies\u201d catches my attention. Martin studied law at university, and qualified as a barrister, but then at 24 bought a pub in north London. There was more than a touch of the maverick, plucky little guy about his business model as he expanded: when brewers refused to sell him their tied-lease pubs, he bought up old banks and shops, licensed the converted premises and set about selling affordable food and drink to the unfashionable masses. Famously scruffy, he drives an old Volvo \u2013 \u201cI don\u2019t own a yacht\u201d \u2013 and is pointedly unimpressed by the pretensions of professional boardroom culture. It makes me wonder if his Euroscepticism is really another expression of his emotional rebellion against the blue-chip elite. \u201cNo. I know what you\u2019re driving at, but no.\u201d It\u2019s the remainers, he claims, who are motivated by emotion. \u201cSomeone from the Financial Times said I was a great amateur philosopher, which is an insult \u2013 and also probably true.\u201d He hoots with laughter. \u201cAnd I think modern people think because they don\u2019t go to church they\u2019re more sophisticated and don\u2019t have a religion. But I think what the EU became was a quasi-religion for that sector of society. And universities are the modern day seminaries.\u201d Although happy to be a philosopher, he is emphatically not a politician. The last time he voted in a general election was 1992; \u201cI\u2019m not proud of that fact. I just didn\u2019t feel strongly enough.\u201d The only other time he has ever voted was in the 80s, for Margaret Thatcher, because she was his local MP. He mispronounces Andrea Leadsom and Gisela Stuart\u2019s names, and until recently Nigel Farage was the only Ukip politician he had even heard of. Were he American, he would vote for Hillary Clinton, and thinks Donald Trump is a \u201ccomplete egocentric weirdo\u201d, but adds that \u201cI trust the American people to look into it in a bit more detail than me,\u201d so will defer to their judgment next week. \u201cI would never want to be a politician. I know my limitations, and I\u2019m sure politics is more difficult than it looks.\u201d Martin\u2019s indignation this week about EU leaders\u2019 hostility towards Britain did seem politically naive. How else did he imagine they would feel about us for leaving? \u201cWell, I didn\u2019t really know. It was impossible to know how they would react.\u201d He was very cross with Juncker for telling EU business leaders to be \u201cintransigent\u201d in their dealings with British companies. \u201cAnd after telling them to make sure the UK pays a price, he has to expect people from the UK who are fighting from the opposite economic position to make the opposite case!\u201d Well, yes, quite. This outbreak of hostilities was precisely what the remainers had warned of. But leavers will be disappointed by his response when I ask about his boycott. \u201cOh dear!\u201d he laughs, burying his head in his hands. \u201cI can see I\u2019m in trouble here already! I haven\u2019t got a boycott. Absolutely not. I was just attempting to show that if European companies do what Juncker says and try to get heavy with us, inevitably we\u2019d go elsewhere.\u201d I don\u2019t know what \u201cget heavy\u201d means. \u201cWell, no one does!\u201d he hoots. Martin has said the UK doesn\u2019t need to sign a trade deal with the EU, because his pubs don\u2019t sign deals with suppliers. He can\u2019t think running the country is like running a pub, can he? \u201cWhat I was saying is that you can\u2019t go into negotiations with the EU insisting upon a particular outcome. If you say your pre-condition is that we have to have free trade, then in order to get that you have to accept what comes with that.\u201d I suggest that I\u2019ll be the EU, and he can be the UK, and he can show me how he would approach the negotiations. \u201cOK, I would say: Decca, I think free trade will suit both our territories better.\u201d I\u2019ll say: fine, but in that case you have to accept free movement of labour. \u201cI\u2019ll say: look, politically, we can\u2019t.\u201d But this is non-negotiable, I\u2019ll say. \u201cThen I think that we have to, er \u2013 well, it\u2019s up to you then, Decca, what you want to do. We\u2019d like to trade with you. You want to trade with us. So what basis would you like to trade on? That\u2019s what I would say.\u201d I\u2019m not sure this would resolve the impasse. Martin is banking on the EU\u2019s aversion to tariffs to overcome its impulse to impose terms that deter other members from leaving \u2013 and he may be right. But this is a businessman\u2019s assumption; to EU leaders, their political project\u2019s survival may turn out to matter more. We move on to much firmer ground when we abandon Europe and talk about the pub trade. To its admirers, Wetherspoon\u2019s is the saviour of the industry \u2013 while nearly 30 pubs go out of business every week, Martin\u2019s chain keeps on growing. With nearly 1,000 pubs, it turns over \u00a31.5bn a year, employs 35,000 staff, and makes Martin, who owns between a quarter and a third of its shares, worth roughly \u00a3250m. But a sign on his office wall reads \u201cOnly the paranoid survive\u201d \u2013 \u201cand I think there\u2019s a lot of truth in that\u201d, he says. Critics accuse the chain of corporate blandness, and he concedes that \u201cthere are instances where the design hasn\u2019t been good enough, and it has given too much of an impression of impersonality or airport lounge. Pubs need individuality.\u201d He spends three days a week visiting his pubs all over the country, and constantly worries about their future. \u201cYou lose a lot without pubs. They\u2019re an important part of the social and cultural life of the country.\u201d But whereas pubs must charge 20% VAT, supermarkets pay \u201cvirtually no VAT on food sales\u201d, allowing them to subsidise drinks prices. \u201cBut most of the big companies say nothing about it. Because the people running them are taking too short-term an approach; they\u2019re there for five years, they\u2019re not looking 10 or 20 years down the line,\u201d Martin says. He worries about millennials binge-drinking cheap supermarket spirits at home, instead of learning \u201csocial drinking\u201d in pubs, which have \u201cmore or less become ghettos now for adults. Some sort of system that allowed 16-year-olds to have a beer or two in a pub would be good.\u201d Martin might not share all of Al Murray\u2019s views about Europe, but he is a big fan. He\u2019s sure it was Murray who assured an anxious post-referendum public: \u201cCrisps will remain at the current price,\u201d and named as his most extravagant possession: \u201cMy wife, and even she\u2019s a bit mean.\u201d In both instances, it was actually him \u2013 which, of course, only makes him laugh again. \u201cShould have got the crisps right.\u201d"
"17||Will the US election mean the end of free trade?There is a tendency to overendow political events with economic significance. However, the 2016 US presidential election could deliver a tectonic shift in international economic relations. For the most part, the candidates\u2019 platforms are standard, partisan fare. Hillary Clinton argues for investment in education and infrastructure, and a higher minimum wage. Donald Trump, too, advocates infrastructure spending along with tax cuts and the repeal of Barack Obama\u2019s healthcare law. However, one issue simultaneously distinguishes the main contenders from previous nominees while separating one from the other. Since at least 1945, it has been a near article of faith in the west that free trade is an engine of growth. No candidate for either main US party has sought to roll back trade liberalisation. Until now. Previously in favour of the Trans-Pacific Partnership (TPP), a draft of which she once described as \u201cthe gold standard in trade agreements\u201d, Hillary Clinton now opposes it. Her reason is that it provides insufficient protection to American workers whose jobs and living standards it might harm. Donald Trump is more strident still, and quite distinctly different from any candidate since 1928. Then, Herbert Hoover won the White House promising farmers that he would raise agricultural tariffs to protect them. In 1930, Congress passed the Smoot-Hawley Tariff Act, which increased levies on a range of goods, not just farm products. The consequent retaliation of other nations intensified the Depression-era slump in world trade. On his visits to Pittsburgh, once the crucible of US steel production, Trump told audiences that the industry\u2019s demise was down to bad trade deals negotiated by incompetent bureaucrats. He promised to conclude good, tough deals and bring back steel. While the likely content of a Trump trade policy is unclear, he has threatened tariffs of 45% on China and 35% on Mexico. He says withdrawing the US from the World Trade Organisation is an option and that he will break or renegotiate the North American Free Trade Agreement. The dogs on the street seem to know that Clinton\u2019s position is an electoral convenience. Pushed by Bernie Sanders, she threw TPP overboard in the primaries but will throw it a lifebelt in office, the argument runs. Rhetorically at least, Trump seems set on holing the world trading system below the waterline. If he expects other countries meekly to accept tariff hikes, he will be sorely disappointed. This antipathy to free trade baffles conventionally schooled policymakers who believe, correctly, that it makes us better off on average. While most people win, however, some lose and lose badly. Economists David Autor, David Dorn and Gordon Hanson have shown that in parts of the US exposed to competition from China, unemployment has risen, pay has fallen and the number claiming benefits has increased. The winners\u2019 gains are spread thinly and widely. The losers\u2019 losses tend to be deep and are often concentrated in specific areas, around cities such as Pittsburgh. Although the winners could compensate the losers leaving everyone better off, neither in the US nor elsewhere have we found a way to do that. Trade adjustment assistance \u2013 payments to US workers affected by import competition \u2013 has not offset the effects of job losses. Electoral college maths makes states such as Ohio and Pennsylvania crucial to the outcome of the race. They more than most have lost jobs to import competition. In this context, railing against trade makes sense. Continuing that in office would leave the US, and others, worse off. Even if Clinton wins and, once in office, tacks towards a freer trade position, this issue will not go away. More Americans believe trade lowers wages and costs jobs than the opposite. This is not just an American phenomenon. In Germany, fewer than two-fifths of people support an EU-US free trade agreement. Among the explanations for the UK\u2019s vote to leave the EU is that the places that voted most for Brexit were those that had lost most in trade with China. Free trade has been a source of economic progress. But winning that case in the public square will no longer be achieved though facts, figures and smart debating points. People need to be convinced, through governments\u2019 actions, that trade agreements will be properly enforced and that when jobs are threatened there is genuine compensation for the losers. Fail to do that and we will undermine one of the most effective means of improving living standards. Stephen Boyle is chief economist at NatWest"
"28||Let\u2019s move to Ottery St Mary, Devon: \u2018It\u2019s fond of its quaint customs\u2019What\u2019s going for it? \u201cTradition has it\u201d: three words to strike terror into the souls of the sane. Tradition has it that on 5 November the people of Ottery St Mary shall run through the streets of the town carrying flaming 30kg barrels of tar above their heads. Why? Why not? Tradition has it. The West Country is full of fire and light festivals as autumn tumbles towards winter. No stranger, after all, than throwing effigies of catholics/Donald Trump on a bonfire. Some say Ottery St Mary\u2019s Guy Fawkes night tradition was to cleanse the streets of evil, or maybe rats. Whatevs. These days it\u2019s all about prowess. Only Ottery families born and bred can take part in the dozens of barrel runs around the town\u2019s old streets from pub to pub. In these days of banned bonfires and \u2019elf and safety gawn mad, we can only applaud this plucky town. Indeed, Ottery is fond of its quaint customs. The parish church peals out the Ottery Song three times a day, and just wait till you hear what the townsfolk get up to with the bellringers on Pixie Day. The case against No butterfingers, please! There are hills in Ottery. A little Wicker Man from this end of the A303. Well connected? Trains: no, you\u2019ll need to head to Exeter. Driving: half an hour to Exeter, 20 mins to the M5. Schools Primaries: Ottery St Mary Primary \u201crequires improvement\u201d, but nearby Tipton St John CofE and Sidbury CofE are \u201cgood\u201d, says Ofsted, with West Hill and Feniton CofE \u201coutstanding\u201d. Secondaries: The King\u2019s School is \u201coutstanding\u201d. Hang out at\u2026 The Rusty Pig \u2013 locally sourced meat, veg and salads, and run like a supper club on Thursdays to Saturdays. Good breakfasts, too. Tickety-Boo coffee shop: cakes, play area. Where to buy Ottery is a predictably pretty affair, with old streets of cottages and town houses huddling round the parish church, its twin transept towers echoing those of Exeter cathedral. There\u2019s a nice line in two-bed, 18th-century cottages. Explore the country lanes and neighbouring villages like Tipton St John. Large detacheds and town houses, \u00a3400,000-\u00a3750,000. Detacheds and smaller town houses, \u00a3220,000-\u00a3400,000. Semis, \u00a3140,000-\u00a3300,000. Terraces and cottages, \u00a3125,000-\u00a3240,000. Rentals: few, a three-bed house might be \u00a3950pcm. Bargain of the week Pretty, double-fronted, two-bed detached house with a lovely little garden, \u00a3169,950, with humberts.com. From the streets Rosina Northcott \u201cI love that it\u2019s a five-minute walk to the countryside yet still only 15-20 minutes to drive to Exeter. A bit of flooding happens every now and again.\u201d Ruud Jansen Venneboer \u201cLovely independent shops, cafes and restaurants. Traffic can get crazy. Not a huge amount for teenagers to do.\u201d \u2022 Live in Ottery St Mary? Join the debate below. Do you live in Rye, East Sussex? Do you have a favourite haunt or a pet hate? If so, email lets.move@theguardian.com by Tuesday 8 November."

"14||Impress dumped as sponsor of the British journalism awardsImpress, the press regulator that secured royal charter recognition, has been dumped as a sponsor of the British journalism awards. Several of the judges warned that they would not be prepared to continue with the judging process if Press Gazette, which organises the annual contest, kept Impress as a sponsor. They are said to have \u201cfelt uncomfortable\u201d at Impress\u2019s involvement and viewed the matter as \u201ca point of principle\u201d. Press Gazette\u2019s editor, Dominic Ponsford, issued a statement on Friday afternoon saying: \u201cA number of the British Journalism Awards judges felt uncomfortable about the fact that Impress were a sponsor of the event. Although the judging process is entirely independent of any sponsorship, they nonetheless felt troubled by their involvement because of their strongly held views on the issue of press regulation. The 49 independent judges are the backbone of the British Journalism Awards and the event cannot happen without their hard work and commitment. We have therefore taken the decision to remove Impress as a sponsor of this year\u2019s awards\u201d. The issue was first raised at a meeting of judges on Thursday. Some judges argued that the choice of Impress, the regulator founded as a rival to the Independent Press Standards Organisation (Ipso), was inappropriate. They viewed Impress as a state-backed body that presents a threat to press freedom. One of them pointed out that Impress does not regulate any of the newspapers being considered for prizes. Ponsford and his editorial team were not responsible for Impress being selected as a sponsor. It was a commercial matter arranged by the magazine\u2019s publisher, Progressive Media Group. Press Gazette is regulated by Ipso and its editorial line has been less than supportive of Impress. The body is largely funded by charities linked to Max Mosley, the former head of Formula One motor racing, who is regarded as anathema by certain editors and publishers. The awards ceremony is due to be staged on 6 December at the Stationers\u2019 Hall in London."
"17||Investor nerves mount as US election draws closer - as it happenedBefore we close up for the day, here is a summary of some of the main developments. US markets opened slightly lower, but are now up. Following eight straight days of losses, Wall Street appears to have been boosted by the non-farm payrolls report. It was a solid jobs report, with no nasty shocks and an unexpected jump in wages. It does raise the prospect of a rate hike in December, but this was already the expectation. If the S&amp;P 500 were to close down (it is currently up 0.2%), it would mark the longest running streak since 1980. Across the Atlantic, European markets are far more jittery about the prospect of a Trump victory at Tuesday\u2019s presidential election. Consumer appetite for new cars has seemed unstoppable in recent years as cheap finance deals and more efficient models have boosted the number of deals. UK new car sales increased again in October, but at a slower rate of 1.4%, compared with 1.6 in September - a plate change month when sales are typically higher. Fleet sales drove the rise in October, while private sales fell 1.1% compared with the same month last year according to the Society of Motor Manufacturers and Traders. A total of 180,168 new cars were registered in the UK last month, taking the total in the first 10 months of the year to 2.3 million - 2.5% higher than at the same point in 2015. Mike Hawes, chief executive of the SMMT, said: September\u2019s number plate change is always a hard act to follow so the market\u2019s growth in October, albeit moderate, is welcome news. If Clinton wins, a rally is on the cards. The headline index edged up to 54.2 from 54.1 in September, in line with expectations. The service sector data follow positive numbers from the manufacturing sector, released earlier in the week. The combined output of both sectors points to the second-strongest rate of expansion so far this year and suggests that the German economy continued to grow at the start of the fourth quarter. Growth slowed in the French services sector in October according to the PMI. The headline index fell to 51.4 from 52.1 on September, missing forecasts that growth would be stable. This was indicative of waning new business growth, as further solid cuts to output prices proved insufficient to prevent the slide. The Italian services sector grew in October, albeit at a weak rate. The headline index on Markit\u2019s PMI rose to 51 from 50.7 in September, where anything above 50 signals expansion. It was a little below expectations of 51.5. This is what Markit\u2019s Phil Smith had to say: It\u2019s been a sluggish start to the fourth quarter for Italy\u2019s economy. While expansion in service sector activity picked up slightly in October, it remained weak overall and was countered by a slowdown in growth of manufacturing output. There was also little to cheer on the employment front, with the rate of job creation across the two sectors combined barely stronger than September\u2019s 12-month low. Growth in Spain\u2019s services sector was roughly stable in October, but the pick up in new orders and employment slowed. "6||Prank call mystery surrounds Ukraine presidentIt was a routine bit of international diplomacy: two presidents speaking for an hour about \u201ctopical issues of the agenda of Ukraine-Kyrgyzstan relations\u201d, as the press release on the website of the Ukrainian president, Petro Poroshenko, put it on Wednesday. Poroshenko asked his counterpart about potential support for a forthcoming Ukrainian initiative at the UN to condemn Russian human rights abuses in annexed Crimea. The only problem is that the Kyrgyz president, Almazbek Atambayev, says he never made the call. Ukrainian authorities are scrambling to work out who Poroshenko spent an hour on the phone with. Suspicion is likely to fall on a pair of Russian hoaxers who have made a habit of embarrassing world leaders with fake calls, and whose pranks often seem to align with Russian state interests. Alexei Stolyarov and Vladimir Kuznetsov, better known as Lexus and Vovan, made the headlines last year when they called Sir Elton John pretending to be the Russian president, Vladimir Putin, and his spokesman, Dmitry Peskov. Putin later called the singer to assuage his embarrassment at falling for the hoax. In April, editors at the New York Times held a conference call with a man claiming to be Poroshenko, after the newspaper received a letter complaining about coverage of Ukraine in its pages. It transpired the call was a hoax. Poroshenko has attracted the attention of the pranksters on numerous occasions, but previously he has been impersonated rather than pranked himself. In February, Stolyarov and Kuznetsov reached the Turkish president, Recep Tayyip Erdo\u011fan, pretending to be Poroshenko. Stolyarov declined to comment when asked by the Guardian whether he was responsible for the fake Kyrgyz call. It is one thing to fool a newspaper or celebrity, and quite another to be able to place hoax calls claiming to be a president calling his counterpart in another country. Such calls are usually set up using elaborate protocol and using special secure lines, prompting many to believe the pranksters must have help from Russia\u2019s FSB security service. Stolyarov and Kuznetsov have denied claims they work for or with the FSB. \u201cWe work for ourselves, nobody else,\u201d Stolyarov told the Guardian in March. The news item about the call was quietly removed from the Ukrainian president\u2019s website, and on Thursday the Kyrgyz and Ukrainian foreign ministers spoke by telephone and attempted to smooth over the incident. A Ukrainian foreign ministry spokeswoman said: \u201cBoth we and the Kyrgyz side are looking into this planned provocation. We will work out all the circumstances, including the technical details. It\u2019s already clear that the goal of this kind of action is to disrupt Ukraine\u2019s important UN initiatives over Crimea.\u201d"
